Introduction and Front Matter

Parsing 'fahak' and the Preposition 'min'
Al-Bajuri explains the particle 'fahak' and the possible meanings of 'min' in the line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the grammatical structure of 'fahak' and identify the two possible meanings of the preposition 'min' in the line.

Core Idea
The particle 'fahak' is not a simple imperative but a compound of 'ha' and the address 'kaf', while 'min' can be explanatory or partitive.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on Ibn Bannani's poem unpacks the opening line. Understanding the grammar of 'fahak' and the ambiguity of 'min' is crucial for correctly interpreting the command to extract rules from the principles of logic. The commentator presents competing analyses, showing how grammatical choices affect meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of 'fahak'
Separate the 'fa' from 'hak'. The 'fa' indicates a suppressed condition (e.g., 'if you intend this art'). The 'hak' is the main subject.
2
Analyze 'hak'
Note the two views: (1) 'hak' as a single word meaning 'take' (ism fi'l, tanbih, or zajr); (2) 'ha' alone as the particle and 'kaf' as a letter of address.
Al-Bajuri favors the second view as correct.
3
Examine the preposition 'min'
In 'min usulihi', 'min' can be explanatory (bayaniyya) meaning 'which are' or partitive (tab'idiyya) meaning 'some of'.
The choice affects whether the rules are identical to the principles or a subset.
4
Consider the implications
If explanatory, the 'qawa'id' are the 'usul' themselves; if partitive, it means 'take some rules from its principles'.
Al-Bajuri notes both possibilities without definitively choosing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (فَهَاكَ ... إلخ) «الفَاءُ لِلإِفْصَاحِ عَنْ شَرْطٍ مَحْذُوفٍ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : إِذَا أَرَدْتَ هَذَا الفَنَّ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ ثَمَرَتَهُ كَذَا وَكَذَا، فَهَاكَ ... إلخ . وَهَاكَ» : اسْمُ فِعْلِ بِمَعْنَى: «خُذْ» ، كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ ابْنُ مَالِكِ فِي التَّسْهِيلِ»، وَذَكَرَ الزَّبِيدِيُّ أَنَّهَا حَرْفُ تَنْبِيهِ (۳) ، وَزَادَ الجَوْهَرِيُّ : أَنَّهَا حَرْفُ زَجْرٍ أَيْضاً ، كَذَا يُؤخَذُ مِمَّا نَقَلَهُ (۳) الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ عَنِ المَكُوَدِيِّ . وَظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ هَاكَ بِتَمَامِهَا اسْمُ فِعْلٍ، أَوْ حَرْفُ تَنْبِيهِ، أَوْ حَرْفُ زَجْرٍ، وَهُوَ أَحَدُ وَجْهَيْنِ؛ ثَانِيَهُمَا - وَهُوَ الرَّاجِعُ : أَنَّ هَا» فَقَط اسْمُ الفِعْلِ، أَوْ حَرْفُ التَّنْبِيهِ ، أَوْ حَرْفُ الزَّجْرِ، وَأَمَّا «الكَافُ» فَحَرْفُ خِطَابِ؛ كَمَا أَفَادَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ. قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ أُصُولِهِ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ مِنْ بَيَانِيَّةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ . وَعَلَى الأَوَّلُ ، فَالمُبَيَّنُ : القَوَاعِدُ المَذْكُورَةُ بَعْدُ عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ الرَّضِيِّ مِنْ جَوَازِ تَقْدِيمِ البَيَانِ عَلَى المُبَيَّنِ، أَوْ شَيْءٍ مَحْذُوفٍ، عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الرَّضِيُّ مِنْ مَنْعِ ذَلِكَ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : فَهَاكَ شَيْئاً مِنْ أُصُولِهِ.
His statement: 'fahak... etc.' The fa is for disclosure of an omitted condition, so the estimation is: 'When you intend this art, having known that its fruit is such and such, then take... etc.' And 'hak' is a noun of action meaning 'take', as Ibn Malik mentioned in al-Tashil, and al-Zabidi mentioned that it is a particle of notification, and al-Jawhari added that it is also a particle of rebuke, as taken from what al-Mallawi narrated from al-Makudi. Its apparent meaning is that 'hak' in its entirety is a noun of action, or a particle of notification, or a particle of rebuke, and this is one of two views. The second – which is the correct one – is that 'ha' alone is the noun of action, or the notification particle, or the rebuke particle, while the 'kaf' is a particle of address, as some investigators have indicated. His statement: 'min usulihi' – it is possible that 'min' is explanatory, and possible that it is partitive. According to the first, the explained is 'the mentioned rules' later, according to the view of other than al-Radi that it is permissible to advance the explanation before the explained, or something omitted, according to al-Radi's view that this is not permissible, and the estimation is: 'take something from its principles.'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line instructing the student to take principles from the foundations of logic. Al-Bajuri first explains the particle 'fahak'. The 'fa' indicates a suppressed condition, e.g., 'if you want this art knowing its benefit, then take...' He then discusses 'hak', which can be interpreted as a noun of action (ism fi'l) meaning 'take', or a notification particle (harf tanbih), or a rebuke particle (harf zajr). He notes two views: one holds that the whole word 'hak' functions as one of these; the other (more correct) holds that only 'ha' is the noun/particle, and the 'kaf' is simply a letter of address (kaf al-khitab). Then he addresses the preposition 'min' in 'min usulihi'. 'Min' could be explanatory (bayaniyya) or partitive (tab'idiyya). If explanatory, it clarifies that the 'qawa'id' (rules) are themselves the 'usul' (principles). If partitive, it indicates taking some of the principles, with the estimated meaning 'take something from its principles'. The choice affects the reading of the text.

Key Terms
فَهَاكَ
هَاكَ
مِن
بَيَانِيَّة
تَبْعِيضِيَّة

D) It is a particle of rebuke

Key Takeaway
The grammatical analysis of 'fahak' and the ambiguity of 'min' in the line illustrate how precise parsing affects the interpretation of a logical text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter

Parsing a Logical Line for Multiple Meanings
Al-Bajuri parses the opening verse of al-Sullam al-Munawraq for grammatical and logical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the possible grammatical interpretations of a given phrase in a logical text and explain how they arise from alternative analyses of each word.

Core Idea
A single line of a logical poem can yield multiple valid interpretations through meticulous grammatical analysis.

In the Islamic intellectual tradition, scholars often unpack dense lines of poetry to reveal layers of meaning. Al-Bajuri's commentary on the opening of al-Sullam al-Munawraq is a classic example: he dissects the phrase 'Fahāka min uṣūlihi qawāʿidan' and shows how different grammatical choices for words like 'hāka', the preposition 'min', and the construct phrase 'uṣūlihi' produce multiple valid readings. This skill—recognizing interpretative possibilities—is essential for anyone working with authoritative texts in logic, law, or theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Locate the main phrase under analysis: 'Fahāka min uṣūlihi qawāʿidan'. This is the opening line of al-Sullam, and each word is scrutinized for alternative grammatical roles.
The phrase translates to 'Take from its principles rules'.
2
Analyze 'hāka'
Notice that 'hāka' is treated in two ways: as a single unit (ism fi‘l, or particle of alerting/deterrence) or as two parts: 'hā' (the verb/particle) and 'kāf' (addressing particle). Al-Bajuri favors the second view.
This distinction affects whether 'hāka' is considered a single lexical item or a compound.
3
Analyze 'min' and the idāfa
Consider the preposition 'min': it can be explanatory (bayāniyya) or partitive (tab‘īḍiyya). Similarly, the idāfa (construct phrase) 'uṣūlihi' can be explanatory (meaning 'which is') or partitive (meaning 'some of').
These two elements independently allow two possibilities each, leading to combinations.
4
Combine the possibilities for 'min' and idāfa
Combine the two interpretations for 'min' and the two for the idāfa: both explanatory, both partitive, 'min' explanatory/idāfa partitive, or the reverse. Al-Bajuri explicitly lists these four.
The four possibilities are not exhaustive of all interpretations but cover the main grammatical options.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Understand that this parsing exercise is not mere pedantry; it demonstrates how a single phrase can support multiple meanings, each with implications for the logical content. Recognizing such possibilities guards against dogmatic readings.
In Islamic sciences, this skill is crucial for interpreting foundational texts and resolving apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (فَهَاكَ ... إلخ) «الفَاءُ لِلإِفْصَاحِ عَنْ شَرْطٍ مَحْذُوفٍ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : إِذَا أَرَدْتَ هَذَا الفَنَّ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ أَنَّ ثَمَرَتَهُ كَذَا وَكَذَا، فَهَاكَ ... إلخ . وَهَاكَ» : اسْمُ فِعْلِ بِمَعْنَى: «خُذْ» ، كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ ابْنُ مَالِكِ فِي التَّسْهِيلِ، وَذَكَرَ الزَّبِيدِيُّ أَنَّهَا حَرْفُ تَنْبِيهِ، وَزَادَ الجَوْهَرِيُّ : أَنَّهَا حَرْفُ زَجْرٍ أَيْضاً ، كَذَا يُؤخَذُ مِمَّا نَقَلَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ عَنِ المَكُوَدِيِّ . وَظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ هَاكَ بِتَمَامِهَا اسْمُ فِعْلٍ، أَوْ حَرْفُ تَنْبِيهِ، أَوْ حَرْفُ زَجْرٍ، وَهُوَ أَحَدُ وَجْهَيْنِ؛ ثَانِيَهُمَا - وَهُوَ الرَّاجِعُ : أَنَّ هَا» فَقَط اسْمُ الفِعْلِ، أَوْ حَرْفُ التَّنْبِيهِ ، أَوْ حَرْفُ الزَّجْرِ، وَأَمَّا «الكَافُ» فَحَرْفُ خِطَابِ؛ كَمَا أَفَادَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ. قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ أُصُولِهِ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ مِنْ بَيَانِيَّةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ . وَعَلَى الأَوَّلُ ، فَالمُبَيَّنُ : القَوَاعِدُ المَذْكُورَةُ بَعْدُ عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ غَيْرُ الرَّضِيِّ مِنْ جَوَازِ تَقْدِيمِ البَيَانِ عَلَى المُبَيَّنِ، أَوْ شَيْءٍ مَحْذُوفٍ، عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ الرَّضِيُّ مِنْ مَنْعِ ذَلِكَ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : فَهَاكَ شَيْئاً مِنْ أُصُولِهِ، وَالإِضَافَةُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : «أُصُولِهِ» يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ تَكُونَ بَيَانِيَّةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ تَكُونَ عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ» التَّنْعِيضِيَّة . وَيَتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا : أَنَّ فِي مِنْ مَعَ الإِضَافَةِ احْتِمَالَاتِ أَرْبَعَةً : (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : كَوْنُهُمَا بَيَانِيَتَيْنِ . (۲) - وَالثَّانِي: كَوْنُهُمَا تَبْعِيضَيّتَيْنِ . (۳) - وَالثَّالِثُ : كَوْنُ مِنْ بَيَانِيَّةً، وَالإِضَافَةُ تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ . ( ٤ ) - وَالرَّابِعُ : العَكْسُ.
His statement: (Fahāka ... etc.) – The 'fa' is for indicating a deleted condition; the estimation is: 'If you want this art, since you know that its fruit is such and such, then take ... etc.' And 'hāka' is a verb of command meaning 'take', as Ibn Mālik mentioned in al-Tashīl, and al-Zabīdī said it is a particle of alerting, and al-Jawharī added that it is also a particle of deterrence, as taken from what al-Mallawī transmitted from al-Makūdī. Its apparent meaning: that 'hāka' in its entirety is a verb of command, or a particle of alerting, or a particle of deterrence, and that is one of two views; the second of them – which is the preponderant – is that only 'hā' is the verb of command, or the particle of alerting, or the particle of deterrence, and as for the 'kāf' it is a particle of address; as stated by some verifiers. His statement: (min uṣūlihi) – it is possible that 'min' is explanatory (bayāniyya), and it is possible that it is partitive (tabʿīḍiyya). On the first, the explained is the rules mentioned later, according to the view of other than al-Raḍī who permit the explanation preceding the explained, or something deleted, according to al-Raḍī's view preventing that, and the estimation is: 'take something from its principles.' And the idāfa in his statement 'uṣūlihi' is possible to be explanatory (bayāniyya), and possible to be in the meaning of partitive 'min'. From this it is deduced that in 'min' together with the idāfa there are four probabilities: (1) The first: both are explanatory. (2) The second: both are partitive. (3) The third: 'min' is explanatory and the idāfa is partitive. (4) The fourth: the reverse.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the opening verse of al-Sullam al-Munawraq, specifically the line 'Fahāka min uṣūlihi qawāʿidan' ('Take from its principles rules'). Al-Bājūrī begins by dissecting the word 'fahāka'. He explains that the 'fa' indicates a condition that is omitted: 'If you want this art... then take'. Then he discusses 'hāka', noting that according to Ibn Mālik it is a verb of command meaning 'take', while al-Zabīdī says it is a particle of alerting, and al-Jawharī adds that it also serves as a particle of deterrence. Al-Bājūrī then presents two views: one treats 'hāka' as a single unit, the other (which he deems preponderant) separates 'hā' as the verb/particle and 'kāf' as a particle of address. Next, he analyzes 'min uṣūlihi'. The preposition 'min' can be either explanatory (bayāniyya, i.e., 'from' meaning 'which is') or partitive (tabʿīḍiyya, i.e., 'some of'). Similarly, the idāfa (construct phrase) 'uṣūlihi' can be explanatory (so 'its principles' = 'the logic') or partitive ('some of its principles'). Combining these, he deduces four possibilities: both explanatory; both partitive; 'min' explanatory and idāfa partitive; or the reverse. This detailed grammatical analysis shows the depth with which scholars approached the text, considering multiple layers of meaning even in a single line.

Key Terms
هاك
من
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bājūrī's analysis, how many possible combinations of interpretations exist for 'min' and the idāfa in the phrase 'min uṣūlihi'?
A) Two: both explanatory or both partitive.
B) Three: both explanatory, both partitive, or mixed.
C) Four: both explanatory, both partitive, min explanatory/idāfa partitive, and the reverse.
D) Five: including interpretations of 'hāka' as distinct.

Key Takeaway
A single line of a logical text can support multiple valid grammatical interpretations, and a scholar must systematically enumerate them to avoid missing important nuances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the phrase 'al-ʿālamu ḥādithun' (the world is temporally originated) from a kalam text. List the possible grammatical interpretations of each word (e.g., can 'al-ʿālam' be a proper noun or a generic term? Is ḥādith a simple predicate or a participle with implications?).
?
Consider the usuli principle: 'al-amru yufīḍu al-wujūb' (a command indicates obligation). If the command is 'aqīmū al-ṣalāta' (establish prayer), what grammatical alternatives could affect the ruling? Use the same method as al-Bājūrī.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Four Grammatical Possibilities in Logical Claims
Commentators explain the grammatical possibilities of the opening line and defend its comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four interpretations of 'min' and idafa in an Arabic logical sentence and determine which interpretation best captures the author's intended scope.

Core Idea
The meaning of a logical claim shifts entirely depending on how you read its particles and constructions.

The opening line of a didactic poem sets the scope of the entire work. The commentators meticulously analyze the grammar to determine exactly what the poet claims to present. In this lesson, we will walk through the four possible interpretations of the particle 'min' and the idafa construction, and see how each affects the scope of the claim. Then we will see how al-Anbabi selects the most plausible reading and defends the poet against an objection of overclaiming.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical elements
Locate the particle 'min' and the idafa construction in the line: 'فَهَاكَ مِنْ أُصُولِهِ قَواعِدًا'. Note that 'min' can be either bayaniyya (clarifying) or tab'idiyya (partitive). Similarly, the idafa in 'أُصُولِهِ' can be bayaniyya (appositional) or tab'idiyya (partitive).
Bayaniyya means the second term specifies the first; tab'idiyya means the second term is a part of the first.
2
List the four combinations
Combine the two possibilities for 'min' with the two for idafa to get four interpretations: (1) both bayaniyya, (2) both tab'idiyya, (3) 'min' bayaniyya and idafa tab'idiyya, (4) 'min' tab'idiyya and idafa bayaniyya.
3
Determine the claimed scope for each
For each interpretation, write what the poet is claiming to present. (1) He presents principles that are entirely identical to the foundations (i.e., all foundations). (2) He presents principles that are a part of the foundations. (3) He presents principles that are a part of the foundations as well. (4) He presents some of the foundations (min tab'idiyya) which are the principles (idafa bayaniyya specifies 'principles' as the foundations themselves).
Al-Anbabi notes that the first three are redundant in meaning, making the fourth the most concise.
4
Evaluate the objection and response
Consider the objection: if the line claims the poem contains all foundations, but it does not. Read al-Anbabi's response: the principles given are the most important, and through them one can grasp the rest, so they are effectively all foundations (a rhetorical hyperbole).
This shows how grammatical analysis interacts with rhetorical interpretation to defend the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الناظم
فَهَاكَ مِنْ أُصُولِهِ قَواعِدًا تَجْمَعُ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ فَوَائِدًا
الباجوري: وعليه فَقَوَاعِدُ : بَدَلٌ مِنْ شَيْءٍ ، أَوْ عَطْفُ بَيَان. والإضافة في قوله : «أُصُولِهِ» يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ تَكُونَ بَيَانِيَّةً، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ تَكُونَ عَلَى مَعْنَى (مِنْ) التَّبْعِيضِيَّة. وَيَتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا : أَنَّ فِي (مِنْ) مَعَ الإِضَافَةِ احْتِمَالَاتٍ أَرْبَعَةً: (1) الأَوَّلُ : كَوْنُهُمَا بَيَانِيَتَيْنِ. (2) وَالثَّانِي: كَوْنُهُمَا تَبْعِيضِيَّتَيْنِ. (3) وَالثَّالِثُ : كَوْنُ (مِنْ) بَيَانِيَّةً، وَالإِضَافَةُ تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ. (4) وَالرَّابِعُ : العَكْسُ.
الأنبابي: قوله : (فَقَوَاعِدُ : بَدَلٌ مِنْ شَيْءٍ) والعامل في البدل حينئذ مقدَّرُ من مادة الأخذ، لا اسم فعل؛ أنَّ اسم الفعل لا يعمل محذوفاً، فإن كان محلُّ المنع ما لم يذكر ما يدلُّ على المحذوف صحّ تقدير اسم الفعل. قوله : (احْتِمَالَاتٍ أَرْبَعَةٌ) أظهرُها رابعها ؛ لِمَا يلزم على غيره مِنَ الزِّيادة المستغنى عنها؛ إذ يكفي على الأوّل: فخذ قواعد هي هو، وعلى الثَّاني: فخذ قواعد هي بعضه، وعلى الثَّالث: فخذ قواعد هي بعضه أيضاً، فإنَّ المعنى على الأوّل : «فَخُذْ قَوَاعِدَ هِيَ أُصُولُ هِيَ هُوَ» . وَأُورِدَ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّهُ لَمْ يَتْرُكْ شَيْئاً مِنْ أُصُولِ المَنْطِقِ، بَلْ ذَكَرَ جَمِيعَهَا فِي هَذَا النَّظْمِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَتْ هَذِهِ القَوَاعِدُ غَالِبَ مُهِمَّاتِ الفَنِّ ، وَبِسَبَبِهَا تَتَحَقَّقُ القُدْرَةُ عَلَى إِدْرَاكِ مَا تَرَكَهُ كَانَتْ كَأَنَّهَا جَمِيعُ أُصُولِهِ.
Introduction of the poet: So take from its foundations principles that gather from its branches benefits. Al-Bajuri: So 'principles' is a substitute for 'something' or a clarifying conjunction. The idafa in his saying 'its foundations' can be of clarification (bayaniyya) or in the sense of 'min' of partition (tab'idiyya). From this it results that in 'min' together with the idafa there are four possibilities: (1) both are for clarification; (2) both are for partition; (3) 'min' is for clarification and the idafa is for partition; (4) the reverse. Al-Anbabi: His saying 'principles is a substitute for something': the operative in the substitution at that point is an implied verb from the root 'taking', not a verbal noun, because a verbal noun does not operate when deleted, unless what indicates the deleted part is mentioned. His saying 'four possibilities': the most apparent is the fourth, because the others incur unnecessary elaboration; since it is sufficient in the first: take principles that are it, in the second: take principles that are some of it, in the third: take principles that are some of it also. So the meaning according to the first is: take principles that are foundations that are it. An objection was raised: that it implies that he left nothing of the foundations of logic, but mentioned all of them in this poem, but it is not so. It was answered: since these principles are most of the important matters of the art, and by them the ability to grasp what he left is achieved, they are as if they are all of its foundations.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is the front matter of the commentary on the opening line of a didactic poem on logic. The commentators analyze the grammatical structure to determine the exact meaning and scope of the poet's claim. First, Al-Bajuri explains that the word 'qawa'id' (principles) is a substitute (badal) or clarifying conjunction ('atf bayan) for the implied 'something' (shay') from the verb 'haka' (take). Then he discusses the idafa construction of 'usulihi' (its foundations): it can be either idafa bayaniyya (appositional, where the second term identifies the first) or idafa tab'idiyya (partitive, where the second term is part of the first). Similarly, the particle 'min' in the line can be bayaniyya (clarifying) or tab'idiyya (partitive). This yields four possible combinations. Al-Anbaji then comments that the operative word in the substitution is an implied verb from 'akhdh' (taking), not an ism fi'l (verbal noun), because an ism fi'l does not function when deleted. He also states that the fourth possibility (where 'min' is partitive and the idafa is clarifying) is the most apparent because the other three involve unnecessary elaboration. To illustrate, he shows that the meanings of the first three can be expressed more simply. Furthermore, an objection is raised that the line would seem to claim the poem covers all foundations of logic, which is false. The answer is that the principles given are most of the important ones, and through them one can grasp the rest, so they are effectively all foundations. This is a common rhetorical device (mubalagha). The passage thus clarifies the poet's intention and the scope of the work.

Key Terms
من البيانية
من التبعيضية
إضافة بيانية
إضافة تبعيضية
قواعد
أصول

Checkpoint

A) Both are bayaniyya
B) Both are tab'idiyya
C) 'Min' bayaniyya and idafa tab'idiyya
D) 'Min' tab'idiyya and idafa bayaniyya

Key Takeaway
The grammatical analysis of particles and idafa in logical texts clarifies the precise scope of the author's claim, and commentators may invoke rhetorical devices to defend apparent overclaims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the sentence 'خذ من أصول الفقه فروعًا' from a hypothetical poem. Enumerate the four possible interpretations of 'min' and the idafa in 'أصول الفقه', and state what each implies about whether the poem covers all or some of the foundations of fiqh.
?
Task 2: In the definition of qiyas, the phrase 'الحاق فرع بأصل' appears. Determine whether the idafa in 'فرع بأصل' is likely bayaniyya or tab'idiyya based on the nature of qiyas (does the branch become identical to the root, or is it attached to it?).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
Grammatical Precision in Logical Matn: The Case of 'Fahaka min Usulihi Qawa'idan'
Grammatical analysis of the opening verse's syntax and semantics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between possible grammatical interpretations of a phrase in an Islamic logic text and identify the logical implications of each reading.

Core Idea
Every word in a logical matn carries precise meaning; the commentator's job is to unlock it.

This lesson examines the opening verse of the matn, where the poet instructs the student to extract rules from principles. The commentators (al-Bajūrī, al-Anbābī, al-Shirshīmī) unpack the grammar to ensure the logical relationship between rules, branches, and benefits is correctly understood. By analyzing pronoun references and the preposition 'min', we see how a small grammatical choice can affect the entire structure of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key grammatical elements
Look at the verse: 'Fahāka min uṣūlihī qawā‘idan tajma‘u min funūnihī fawā’idā.' Identify the verbal noun 'hāka', its object 'qawā‘idan', the verb 'tajma‘u', and its object 'fawā’idā'. Notice the two occurrences of 'min'.
The verse uses poetic license; standard prose would differ.
2
Analyze the pronoun in 'tajma‘u'
The commentator notes two possibilities: the pronoun could refer to 'al-qawā‘id' (the rules themselves gather benefits) or to the addressee (the student gathers benefits by means of the rules). Each reading changes the agency.
The immediate understanding favors the rules as the subject.
3
Distinguish the three possible meanings of 'min' in 'min funūnihi'
The commentator lists three interpretations: explanatory (bayāniyya), where 'funūn' clarifies 'fawā’id'; partitive (tab‘īḍiyya), where benefits are some of the branches; and initiational (ibtidā’iyya), where benefits come from the branches. Each has different logical consequences.
The partitive reading avoids identity between rules (collector) and benefits (collected).
4
Assess the logical implications
If 'funūn' meant sciences (types), then rules would be identical to benefits, causing confusion. The commentator insists 'funūn' means particular sub-rules derived from the universal rules, preserving distinct levels of generality.
This mirrors the relationship between universals and particulars in logic.

قَوْلُهُ : (قَوَاعِدَا) مَفْعُولٌ بِهِ لِاسْمِ الفِعْلِ، وَالقَوَاعِدُ جَمْعُ : «قَاعِدَةٍ ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ تَعْرِيفُهَا ، فَتَنَبَّه . قَوْلُهُ : (تَجْمَعُ ... إلخ) المُتَبَادَرُ : أَنَّ الضَّمِيرَ رَاجِعٌ لِـ «القَوَاعِدِ» . وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهُ لِلمُخَاطَبِ أَوَّلاً فِي قَوْلِهِ : «فَهَاكَ ... إلخ»، وَالمَعْنَى عَلَيْهِ : «أَنَّكَ تَجْمَعُ بِسَبَبٍ تِلْكَ القَوَاعِدِ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ . .. إلخ». قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ فُنُونِهِ يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ مِنْ بَيَانِيَّةُ لِـ «الفَوَائِدِ المَذْكُورَةِ بَعْدُ، أَوْ لِشَيْءٍ مَحْذُوفٍ عَلَى الخِلَافِ المُتَقَدِّمِ بَيْنَ الرَّضِيِّ وَغَيْرِهِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ ، وَالمُرَادُ بـ «الفُنُونِ» : الفُرُوعُ الجُزْنِيَّةُ المُسْتَفَادَةُ مِنَ القَوَاعِدِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، : وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ بِهَا مَا هُوَ ظَاهِرُهَا مِنَ الأَنْوَاعِ ; لِئَلَّا يَتَّحِدَ الجَامِعُ وَالمَجْمُوعُ، أَوِ السَّبَبُ فِي الجَمْعِ وَمُتَعَلَّقُ المُسَبَّبِ، وَهُوَ المَجْمُوعُ عَلَى الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ فِي الضَّمِيرِ الَّذِي فِي قَوْلِهِ : «تَجْمَعُ، وَوَجْهُ الاتِّحَادِ حِينَئِذٍ : أَنَّ الأَنْوَاعَ هِيَ القَوَاعِدُ ، وَالفَوَائِدَ المَجْمُوعَةَ هِيَ الأَنْوَاعُ أَوْ بَعْضُهَا عَلَى الاحْتِمَالَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ فِي مِنْ». نَعَمْ؛ إِنْ جَعَلْتَ مِنْ ابْتِدَائِيَّةٌ لَمْ يَلْزَمِ الاتِّحَادُ ; لأنَّ الفَوَائِدَ المَجْمُوعَةَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَتْ
His saying: (qawā‘idā) is an object of the verbal noun, and 'al-qawā‘id' is the plural of 'qā‘ida', and its definition has preceded, so pay attention. His saying: (tajma‘u ... etc.) The immediate understanding is that the pronoun refers to 'al-qawā‘id'. And it is possible that it refers to the addressee first in his saying 'fahāka ... etc.', and the meaning upon it is: 'that you gather by means of those rules from its branches ... etc.' His saying: (min funūnihi) is possibly explanatory (bayāniyya) of 'al-fawā‘id' mentioned afterwards, or of something omitted according to the earlier disagreement between al-Raḍī and others. And it is possible that it is partitive (tab‘īḍiyya), and the intended meaning by 'al-funūn' is the particular branches derived from the universal rules, and not the apparent meaning of the types, lest the collector and the collected become identical, or the cause of collection and the object of the effect, which is the collected, according to the two possibilities in the pronoun in his saying 'tajma‘u'. And the aspect of identity then is that the types are the rules, and the collected benefits are the types or some of them according to the two possibilities in 'min'. Yes, if you make 'min' initiational (ibtidā’iyya), identity does not necessarily follow, because the collected benefits then are not
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the opening verse of the matn (text to be memorized). The commentator (al-Bajūrī) and marginal annotators (al-Anbābī, al-Shirshīmī) unpack the grammar and semantics. The verse is: 'So take from its principles rules that gather from its branches benefits.' The commentator first notes that 'qawā‘idā' is the object of the verbal noun 'hāka' (take). He reminds that 'qawā‘id' (rules) has been defined earlier. Then he discusses the pronoun in 'tajma‘u' (gathers): the immediate understanding is it refers back to 'al-qawā‘id' (the rules), but it could also refer to the addressee (the student), meaning 'you gather by means of those rules from its branches benefits'. Next, he analyzes the preposition 'min' in 'min funūnihi'. It could be explanatory (bayāniyya) of the benefits, or partitive (tab‘īḍiyya). If partitive, then 'funūn' (branches) are the particular sub-rules derived from the universal rules, not the sciences themselves, to avoid the collector (the rules) being identical with the collected (the benefits). The commentator also notes a previous disagreement between al-Raḍī and others regarding a similar grammatical point. He explains that if 'min' is taken as initiational (ibtidā’iyya), then identity of collector and collected does not arise. The discussion aims to clarify the precise logical relationship between principles, rules, branches, and benefits.

Key Terms

فَنّ (ج. فُنُون)
مِنْ بَيَانِيَّة
مِنْ تَبْعِيضِيَّة

According to the commentary, why does the commentator reject interpreting 'funūn' as 'sciences' (anwā‘)?
A) Because it would contradict the dictionary definition.
B) Because it would cause the collector (rules) and the collected (benefits) to be identical.
C) Because it would make 'min' partitive.
D) Because it would make the pronoun refer to the rules.

Key Takeaway
Grammatical choices in a logical text are not arbitrary; they safeguard the precise hierarchical relationship between universals and particulars.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Parsing Logical Relations in al-Sullam's Opening Line
Commentator analyzes the syntax and logical relationships in the poem's first line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible interpretations of 'min' and the idafa in the opening line of al-Sullam al-Munawraq and explain why the commentator rejects the interpretation that leads to identity between rules and benefits.

Core Idea
Avoiding logical identity between rules and benefits requires grammatically precise interpretation.

The opening line of al-Sullam al-Munawraq may seem straightforward, but its logical structure contains subtle relationships that demand careful parsing. Al-Bajuri's commentary unpacks the multiple possible meanings of 'min' and the construct phrase to ensure that the principles, rules, branches, and benefits are properly distinguished without conflation. This lesson walks through his analytical process, showing how grammatical choices affect logical coherence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the poetic line and key terms
Locate the terms 'usul', 'qawa'id', 'funun', and 'fawa'id' in the Arabic. Note their literal meanings and the relationships implied by the particles and construct phrases.
2
Understand the two possibilities for 'min'
Recognize that 'min' can be explanatory (bayaniyya) or partitive (tab'idiyya). If explanatory, 'funun' are identical to 'fawa'id'; if partitive, 'funun' are a portion of 'fawa'id'.
These possibilities affect whether the rules and benefits are the same or distinct.
3
Recognize the problem of identity
If 'funun' are interpreted as the types (anwa') that are the rules themselves, then the collector (qawa'id) would be identical to the collected (fawa'id). Al-Bajuri explicitly rejects this because it creates logical confusion.
This shows the commentator's concern with avoiding conceptual conflation.
4
Consider the third possibility: 'min' as starting point
If 'min' is ibda'iyya (starting point), then the collected benefits are not the types but the particular subdivisions derived from them, thus escaping identity.
This interpretation maintains a clear distinction between rules and their instantiations.
5
Examine the idafa (construct phrase) possibilities
Al-Bajuri notes that the idafa 'fununihi' can be either 'of the dependent to the thing depended on' (if mantiq refers only to universal issues) or explanatory (if mantiq includes particular branches). These possibilities combine with the 'min' interpretations to yield four total readings.
This step illustrates how multiple grammatical layers interact in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَهَاكَ مِنْ أُصُولِهِ قَواعِدًا تَجْمَعُ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ فَوَائِدًا) قال الباجوري: وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهُ لِلمُخَاطَبِ أَوَّلاً. وَالمَعْنَى عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّكَ تَجْمَعُ بِسَبَبٍ تِلْكَ القَوَاعِدِ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ. قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ فُنُونِهِ يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ مِنْ بَيَانِيَّةُ لِـ «الفَوَائِدِ» المَذْكُورَةِ بَعْدُ، أَوْ لِشَيْءٍ مَحْذُوفٍ عَلَى الخِلَافِ المُتَقَدِّمِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ، وَالمُرَادُ بـ «الفُنُونِ» : الفُرُوعُ الجُزْنِيَّةُ المُسْتَفَادَةُ مِنَ القَوَاعِدِ الكُلِّيَّةِ. وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ بِهَا مَا هُوَ ظَاهِرُهَا مِنَ الأَنْوَاعِ؛ لِئَلَّا يَتَّحِدَ الجَامِعُ وَالمَجْمُوعُ، أَوِ السَّبَبُ فِي الجَمْعِ وَمُتَعَلَّقُ المُسَبَّبِ، وَهُوَ المَجْمُوعُ عَلَى الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ فِي الضَّمِيرِ الَّذِي فِي قَوْلِهِ : تَجْمَعُ، وَوَجْهُ الاتِّحَادِ حِينَئِذٍ : أَنَّ الأَنْوَاعَ هِيَ القَوَاعِدُ، وَالفَوَائِدَ المَجْمُوعَةَ هِيَ الأَنْوَاعُ أَوْ بَعْضُهَا عَلَى الاحْتِمَالَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ فِي مِنْ. نَعَمْ؛ إِنْ جَعَلْتَ مِنْ ابْتِدَائِيَّةٌ لَمْ يَلْزَمِ الاتِّحَادُ؛ لأنَّ الفَوَائِدَ المَجْمُوعَةَ حِينَئِذٍ لَيْسَتْ هِيَ الأَنْوَاعُ حَقِيقَةٌ، بَلِ الفُرُوعُ المُبْتَدَأَةُ مِنْهَا. وَعَلَى كَوْنِ المُرَادِ بـ «الفُنُونِ» الفُرُوعَ الجُزْنِيَّةَ تَكُونُ الإِضَافَةُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : فُنُونِهِ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُتَعَلِّقِ لِلمُتَعَلَّقِ إِنْ كَانَ المَنْطِقُ لَا يُطْلَقُ إِلَّا عَلَى المَسَائِلِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الفُرُوعِ الجُزْيَّةِ أَيْضاً كَانَتْ مِنَ الإِضَافَةِ البَيَانِيَّةِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ التَّبْعِيضِيَّةِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا يَتَحَصَّلُ أَنَّ فِي مِنْ مَعَ الإِضَافَةِ أَرْبَعَةُ احْتِمَالاَتٍ.
His statement: 'So take from its principles rules that collect from its branches benefits.' Al-Bajuri said: It is possible that it is for the addressee first. The meaning according to this: that you collect, because of those rules, from its branches. His statement: 'from its branches'—it is possible that 'min' is explanatory for 'the benefits' mentioned later, or for something omitted according to the earlier disagreement. And it is possible that it is partitive, and by 'the branches' is meant the particular subdivisions derived from the universal rules. It is not meant by them what is apparent of them, i.e., the types, so that the collector and the collected do not become identical, or the cause of collection and the object of the effect, which is the collected, according to the two possibilities in the pronoun in his statement: 'you collect.' The aspect of identity then is that the types are the rules, and the collected benefits are the types or some of them, according to the two possibilities mentioned for 'min.' Yes, if you make 'min' starting (ibda'iyya), identity is not necessary, because the collected benefits then are not the types in reality, but the subdivisions derived from them. And on the interpretation that by 'the branches' is meant the particular subdivisions, the idafa in his statement: 'its branches' is of the idafa of the dependent to the thing depended on, if 'mantiq' is applied only to the universal issues; but if it is also applied to the particular subdivisions, then it is of the explanatory idafa. And it is possible that it is on the meaning of partitive 'min,' and on this there results that in 'min' with the idafa there are four possibilities.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical-logical analysis of the opening line of al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The commentator, al-Bajuri, examines the phrase 'فهاك من أصوله قواعد تجمع من فنونه فوائدا' to clarify the relationships between its key terms: usul (principles), qawa'id (rules), funun (branches), and fawa'id (benefits). He first notes that the verb 'فهاك' (take) may be addressed to an implied interlocutor, meaning 'you take from its principles rules that collect benefits from its branches.' The central issue is the particle 'min' (from) and the idafa (construct phrase) 'من فنونه'. Al-Bajuri states that 'min' could be bayaniyya (explanatory), meaning that the branches are the benefits themselves, or it could be tab'idiyya (partitive), meaning some branches. If 'min' is explanatory, then 'funun' (branches) are interpreted as the particular subcases (furu' juz'iyya) derived from the universal rules (qawa'id kulliyya), not as the types (anwa') that are the rules themselves. This avoids the problem of the collector (qawa'id) being identical to the collected (fawa'id). If 'min' is partitive, then the branches are part of the benefits, meaning some benefits are collected. Al-Bajuri further argues that if 'min' is ibda'iyya (starting point), identity is avoided because the collected benefits are the derivatives (furu') rather than the rules. He then discusses the idafa: if 'mantiq' refers only to universal principles, then the idafa is 'of the dependent to the thing depended on' (i.e., the branches depend on the principles). If 'mantiq' also includes particular branches, then the idafa is explanatory (bayaniyya). Finally, he notes that these possibilities combine to yield four total interpretations of 'min' and the idafa. This careful parsing demonstrates the commentator's concern with precise logical relationships and prevention of conceptual conflation.

قواعد
فنون
فوائد

According to al-Bajuri's analysis, what problem arises if 'funun' in the line 'تجمع من فنونه فوائدا' is interpreted as the types (anwa') that are identical to the rules (qawa'id)?
A) The benefits would be impossible to collect.
B) The collector (qawa'id) and the collected (fawa'id) would become identical.
C) The particle 'min' would lose its meaning.
D) The word 'funun' would be redundant.

Key Takeaway
Precise grammatical analysis of particles and construct phrases is essential in Islamic logic to prevent conflation of distinct conceptual levels, such as universal rules and their particular benefits.

Introduction and Front Matter

Meaning of 'Sullam' and Preferred View on Name Reference
Al-Bajuri defines 'sullam' and discusses the preferred interpretation of the book's name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three linguistic senses of 'sullam' in this passage and explain the preferred view on what the book's name refers to, including the condition of observing meanings.

Core Idea
The book's name refers to words, but only when meanings are intentionally considered.

This lesson unpacks the opening of al-Bajuri's commentary on the Sullam al-Murawnaq. You will see how the commentator navigates multiple possible interpretations of what the title refers to—words, meanings, inscriptions, or combinations thereof—and then settles on the preferred view. You will also learn how the word 'sullam' (ladder) shifts from a literal physical object to a metaphorical one, and finally to a proper name that is linguistically real. Understanding this helps you appreciate how logicians define their technical terms with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage asks: what exactly does the name 'Sullam al-Murawnaq' refer to? Look for the phrase 'فِي مُسَمَّى الكُتُبِ' (regarding what is meant by 'books').
2
List the five possible views
The author enumerates five possibilities: words only, meanings only, inscriptions only, two of the three, or all three. Note that the Arabic uses 'أَوِ' to present them as alternatives.
These are the logical options for what a name can signify.
3
Find the preferred view
The text states 'وَالمُخْتَارُ الأَوَّلُ' (the preferred is the first), but adds a crucial condition: 'لَكِنْ بِقَيْدِ مُلَاحَظَةِ المَعَانِي' (but with the condition of observing the meanings). This means the name refers to words, but only when they are considered together with their meanings.
The condition prevents the name from referring to empty sounds.
4
Analyze the word 'sullam' linguistically
Al-Bajuri distinguishes three senses: (1) literal: a physical ladder for climbing; (2) metaphorical: used here for the science of logic; (3) proper name: as a title, it is a transferred proper noun (علم منقول), which is considered real (حقيقة) by linguistic assignment.
This shows how a word can have multiple layers of meaning.
5
Understand the condition's necessity
The marginal commentator al-Anbabi explains that while words are transient accidents (أعراض منقضية), attaching meanings to them makes them strong enough to serve as valid designations (مدلول). This reflects the Ash'ari theory of signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَمَّيْتُهُ بِـ: «السُّلَّمِ المُرَوْنَقِ» يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ
فِي مُسَمَّى الكُتُبِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهُ الأَلْفَاظُ فَقَط ، أوِ المَعَانِي فَقَطْ ، أَوِ النُّقُوسُ فَقَدْ ، أَوِ اثْنَانِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الثَّلَاثَةِ، أَوْ مَجْمُوعُ الثَّلَاثَةِ .
وَالمُخْتَارُ الأَوَّلُ ، لَكِنْ بِقَيْدِ مُلَاحَظَةِ المَعَانِي كَمَا عَلِمْتَ .
قَوْلُهُ : (بِالسُّلَّم) مَفْعُولٌ ثَانٍ لِـ «سَمَّى»، وَأَدْخَلَ البَاءَ عَلَيْهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَجُوزُ تَعَدِّيهِ إِلَيْهِ بِهَا ، كَمَا يَجُوزُ تَعَدِّيهِ إِلَيْهِ بِنَفْسِهِ؛ تَقُولُ : سَمَّيْتُ ابْنِي بِمُحَمَّدٍ، وَسَمَّيْتُهُ مُحَمَّد .
وَالسُّلَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ فِيمَا يُتَوَصَّلُ بِهِ إِلَى أَعْلَى، إِذَا كَانَ ذَلِكَ الأَمْرُ مَحْسُوساً بِحَاسَةِ البَصَرِ، وَإِلَّا كَانَ مجازاً بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ التَّصْرِيحِيَّةِ كَمَا هُنَا ، لَكِنْ جَعَلَهُ هُنَا مجازاً بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ العَلَمِيَّةِ، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ حَقِيقَةٌ لِوَضْعِهِ عَلَى هَذَا المَتْنِ بِطَرِيقِ النَّقْلِ ، لَا لِمَا قِيلَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ صَارَ حَقِيقَةٌ عُرْفِيَّةٌ فِيهِ، فَهُوَ مِنَ الأَعْلَامِ المَنْقُولَةِ، وَهِيَ حَقَائِقُ .

الأنبابي
قوله : (وَالمُخْتَارُ الأَوَّلُ) أي: وهو الألفاظ.
وقوله : (لَكِنْ بِقَيْدِ مُلَاحَظَةِ المَعَانِي) أي: فهي شرط، فإنَّ الألفاظ وإن كانت أعراضاً منقضية، لكن لمَّا ضمَّ إليها اعتبار المعاني تقوَّت وصلحت لأن تكون مدلولاً.
I named it 'The Adorned Ladder' by which the sky of the science of logic is ascended. Regarding what is meant by 'books', he said: It is possible that it is the words only, or the meanings only, or the inscriptions only, or two of these three, or the sum of the three. The preferred view is the first, but with the condition of observing the meanings, as you have learned. His statement: 'with the ladder' is the second object of 'named', and he inserted the preposition 'with' because it is permissible for it to be transitive with it, just as it is permissible for it to be transitive without it; you say: 'I named my son with Muhammad' and 'I named him Muhammad'. The word 'ladder' is literal with respect to that by which one reaches a higher place, when that thing is perceived by the sense of sight; otherwise, it is metaphorical by explicit metaphor, as here. However, here he considered it metaphorical by disregarding its being a proper name; otherwise, it is literal by virtue of its being assigned to this text by way of transfer, not because of what is said that it became a customary literal term for it, so it is of the transferred proper nouns, and these are real. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'The preferred view is the first' means that it is the words. And his statement: 'but with the condition of observing the meanings' means that it is a condition; for although the words are transient accidents, when consideration of the meanings is attached to them, they become strong and suitable to be a designation.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the opening line of the matn 'Sullam al-Murawnaq'. The commentator first explains the possible interpretations of what the name of the book refers to: it could be just the words, just the meanings, just the inscriptions, any two of these three, or all three. He then states the preferred view: the name refers to the words, but with the crucial condition that the meanings are observed. This condition is necessary because words alone are transient; attaching meanings gives them substance. Next, the commentator examines the word 'sullam' (ladder). Grammatically, it is the second object of 'named' with the preposition 'bi'. He notes that 'sullam' is used literally for a physical ladder, but here it is metaphorical, as the science of logic is not physically ascended. However, because 'Sullam al-Murawnaq' is a proper name for this book, it is actually a transferred proper noun (علم منقول), which is considered literal in the sense of linguistic assignment. The marginal annotator, al-Anbabi, confirms that the preferred choice is the words, and he elaborates on why the condition of observing meanings is necessary: despite words being mere accidents, when they are combined with the consideration of meanings, they become strong enough to serve as a designation. This reasoning reflects the standard Ash'ari perspective on the nature of linguistic signification.

Key Terms
السلم
المروّنق
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred view (al-mukhtar) regarding what the name of the book 'Sullam al-Murawnaq' refers to?
A) The words only
B) The meanings only
C) The inscriptions only
D) The words with the condition of observing the meanings

Key Takeaway
The name of a book can refer to words, but only when meanings are intentionally considered, and a word like 'sullam' can be literal, metaphorical, or a proper noun depending on context.

Analyze the title of another canonical logic text, such as 'Isagoge' (المدخل) or 'Kitab al-Burhan'. Determine, based on the principles from this lesson, whether its name refers to words, meanings, or both, and what condition might be attached.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Book Names vs. Science Names: Individual or Generic Proper Nouns?
Al-Bajuri distinguishes book names as 'alam shakhs from science names as 'alam jins, with a dissenting view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify names of books and sciences as either 'alam shakhs or 'alam jins and explain the rationale behind each classification.

Core Idea
Book names are individual proper names; science names are generic proper names — unless you side with the verifiers.

In this passage, al-Bajuri tackles a subtle point in Arabic morphology: whether the names of books and sciences are proper names of individuals or generic proper names. This distinction may seem pedantic, but it has implications for how we understand the unity of a discipline. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of proper nouns
In Arabic, proper nouns (a‘lām) are divided into 'alam shakhṣ (proper name of an individual) and 'alam jins (generic proper name). Understand the difference: 'alam shakhṣ refers to a unique specific entity; 'alam jins refers to a species or universal essence.
For example, 'Zayd' is 'alam shakhṣ; 'Usāmah' (as a name for lion-kind) is 'alam jins.
2
Apply 'alam shakhṣ to book names
Al-Bajuri states that book names (and biography titles) are 'alam shakhṣ because they denote a specific set of words conveying specific meanings — a determinate entity. The existence of multiple copies does not make it a genus; that is a philosophical subtlety ignored by Arabic linguists.
He cites al-‘Iṣām's commentary on the Treatise on Convention for support.
3
Apply 'alam jins to science names (well-known view)
Science names like 'al-nahw' or 'al-fiqh' are classified as 'alam jins according to the majority. The referent is the universal essence of the rules of that science, including both actual and potential knowledge.
This view treats science names as similar to 'Usāmah' for lions.
4
Examine the dissenting view
Some verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) argue that science names are also 'alam shakhṣ because the named entity is the specific rulings (aḥkām) of that science, which form a determinate concept. Al-Anbabi explains that the coiner conceptualizes the precise rules as a unified whole.
The dissenting view is not majority but is noted as a valid alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ أَسْمَاءَ الكُتُبِ - وَمِثْلَهَا أَسْمَاءُ التَّرَاجِمِ - مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ؛ لِأَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهَا - الَّذِي هُوَ الأَلْفَاظُ المَخْصُوصَةُ الدَّالَّة عَلَى المَعَانِي المَخْصُوصَةِ - مُشَخَصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَحَلِّ ; لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَنْشَأ عَنِ التَّدْقِيقِ الفَلْسَفِيّ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَر عِنْدَ أَرْبَابِ العَرَبِيَّةِ كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ العصامُ فِي شَرْحِ رِسَالَةِ الوَضْعِ، بِخِلَافِ أَسْمَاءِ العُلُومِ، فَإِنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الجِنْسِ عَلَى المَشْهُورِ،
لَكِنِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ أَيْضاً، لأَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ الأَحْكَامُ المَخصُوصَةُ مُشَخَصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَحَلِّ كَمَا ذُكِرَ، فَلْيُفهم .

الأنبابي
قوله : (لَكِنِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ ... إلخ) أي: لأنَّ مدلولها القواعد المضبوطة الحاصلة بالفعل أو بالقوَّة، فالواضع استحضرها بجهة وحدتها ووضع عليها الاسم المخصوص.
قوله : (وَلَا نَظَرَ لِتَعَدُّدِهِ بِتَعَدُّدِ المَحَلِّ) قد يقال : القول بأنَّ أسماء العلوم مِن قبيل أعلام الأجناس ليس مبنيا على أنَّ الشَّيء يتعدد بتعدد محله الذي هو تدقيق فلسفي، بل لأنَّه لما كانت أسماء العلوم أسماء قابلة للزيادة؛ لأنَّها تزيد بزيادة العلماء كانت أسماء لماهيَّات القواعد الكلية الشاملة، تلك الماهيات لما حصل بالفعل ولما لم يحصل منها كـ «أسامة» اسم للماهيَّة الشاملة لما حصل مِنَ الإفراد بالفعل ولما لم يحصل، بخلاف أسماء الكتب والتراجم، فإنَّها أسماء لأشياء لا تقبل الزيادة؛ إذ هي للأفراد الحاصلة بالفعل.
And know that names of books – and likewise names of biographies (tarājim) – are of the type of proper name of an individual ('alam shakhṣ), because what is named by them – which is the specific words indicating specific meanings – is a particular, determinate entity, and no consideration is given to its multiplicity by multiplicity of place, for that arises only from philosophical subtlety, which is not taken into account by the masters of Arabic, as al-‘Iṣām verified in his commentary on the Treatise on Convention (Risālat al-Waḍ‘), unlike names of sciences, for they are of the type of generic proper name ('alam jins) according to the well-known view. However, some verifying scholars chose that they are also of the type of proper name of an individual, because what is named by them – which are the specific rulings (aḥkām) – is a particular, determinate entity, and no consideration is given to its multiplicity by multiplicity of place, as was mentioned, so let that be understood. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'However, some verifying scholars chose... etc.' means that their referent is the precise rules that occur actually or potentially, so the coiner brings them to mind as a unified whole and places the specific name upon them. His statement: 'And no consideration is given to its multiplicity by multiplicity of place' – it may be said that the statement that names of sciences are of the type of generic proper names is not based on the idea that a thing is multiplied by the multiplicity of its place, which is a philosophical subtlety, but rather because since names of sciences are names that admit of increase – for they increase with the increase of scholars – they are names for the essences of comprehensive universal rules, those essences including what has actually been obtained and what has not been obtained from them, like 'Usāmah', which is a name for the comprehensive essence including both what has actually been obtained of individuals and what has not, unlike names of books and biographies, for they are names for things that do not admit of increase, since they are for individuals that have actually been obtained.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a classification of proper nouns (a‘lām) as applied to book titles and science names. The author begins by stating that book names, like the titles of specific works and personal biographies, are proper names of individuals ('alam shakhṣ). This is because what they denote – a specific set of words conveying specific meanings – is a unique, determinate entity. The fact that multiple copies of the same book exist does not change this individuality; such concerns are dismissed as 'philosophical subtlety' (tadqīq falsafī) irrelevant to Arabic linguistic convention. This is attributed to al-‘Iṣām in his commentary on the Treatise on Convention. In contrast, science names (e.g., 'al-nahw', 'al-fiqh') are, according to the well-known view, generic proper names ('alam jins), like 'Usāmah' which names the species of lions, not a particular lion. However, a group of verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) – including likely al-Dawwānī – argue that science names are also 'alam shakhṣ, because what they name are the specific rulings (aḥkām) of that science, which are also a determinate concept. The annotator al-Anbabi expands on this: science names denote the precise rules (qawā‘iḍ maḍbūṭa) that exist actually or potentially; the coiner conceptualizes them as a unified set and assigns a name. The argument for the generic view is not based on multiplicity by place but on the fact that sciences grow with new discoveries, so the name refers to a universal essence that includes both actual and potential rules, analogous to 'Usāmah' as a generic proper name for all lions actual or potential. Book names, however, are for specific, actual individuals and do not admit of such increase.

Key Terms
علم الشخص
علم الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) As proper names of individuals (‘alam shakhṣ)
B) As generic proper names (‘alam jins)
C) As common nouns (ism jins)
D) As not being proper names at all

Key Takeaway
The classification of a proper noun as ‘alam shakhṣ or ‘alam jins depends on whether its referent is a unique individual or a universal essence.

Transmitted vs. Common Pronunciation: The Title 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq'
Author discusses the title 'al-Murawnaq' and its transmitted variant 'al-Munawraq'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between transmitted and common pronunciations of a technical term and evaluate which is preferred based on textual evidence and aesthetic reasoning.

Core Idea
Transmitted pronunciation trumps common usage even when the common is widespread.

When studying classical Islamic texts, you will often encounter variant pronunciations. This lesson examines a concrete example from the title of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. We will see how the commentator prefers the transmitted version over the famous one, using criteria that are widely applicable in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two variants
Find the two pronunciations of the book title: al-Murawnaq (ra before waw) and al-Munawraq (nun before waw). The commentator states which is common and which is transmitted from the author.
2
Understand the criteria for preference
Al-Bajuri gives two reasons for preferring al-Munawraq: it is the transmitted version from the author, and it has a pleasing rarity (gharaba) that makes it 'good and sweet'.
Transmission (riwaya) is the primary criterion; aesthetic appeal is secondary.
3
Examine the poetic evidence
A poet is cited to support the common pronunciation. The commentator refutes this by showing that the poet's actual words use 'nawraq' (nun before waw), not 'murawnaq'.
Always verify citations against the original transmission.
4
Apply the same method to a new example
Consider a term like 'al-hamdulillah' vs. 'al-hamdu lillah'. Which is transmitted? How would you decide?
Use the principles: transmission first, then aesthetic or poetic consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (المُرَوْنَقِ) بِتَقْدِيمِ الرَّاءِ عَلَى الوَاءِ، وَتَأخِيرِ النُّونِ عَنْهُمَا كَمَا اشْتُهِرَ لَكِنَّ المَرْوِيَّ عَنِ المُصَنِّفِ : المُنَورَقِ بِتَقْدِيمِ النُّونِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ الرَّاءِ عَنْهُمَا ، وَهُمَا وَإِنْ كَانَا بِمَعْنًى وَاحِدٍ وَهُوَ : «المُزَيَّنُ المُزَخَّرَفُ، لَكِنْ ثَانِيهَا أَوْلَى؛ لِكَوْنِهِ هُوَ الرِّوَايَةُ، وَلِكَوْنِهِ حَسَناً عَذْباً بِسَبَبٍ غَرَابَتِهِ وَعَدَمِ جَرَيَانِهِ عَلَى الأَلْسِنَةِ، بِخِلَافِ أَوَّلِهِمَا . وَقَدِ اسْتَشْهَدَ بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ بِقَوْلِ الشَّاعِرِ حِينَ سُئِلَ هَلْ خَطُّ المَلِكِ أَحْسَنُ أَوْ خَطُ ابْنِ مُقْلَة؟ بَعْدَ أَنْ رَأَى الخَطَّيْنِ : يَخُطُّ مَوْلَانَا خُطُوطَ ابْنِ مُقْلَة وَيُنَظِمُهَا نَظمَ اللَّآلِي فِي السِّلْكِ فَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَطَّ وَحْدَه وَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَط وَالمَلِك وَرُدَّ ذَلِكَ الاِسْتِشْهَادُ بِأَنَّ المَرْوِيَّ عَنِ الشَّاعِرِ نَوْرَقُ بِتَقْدِيمِ النُّونِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ الرَّاءِ عَنْهُمَا ، لَا بِتَقْدِيمِ الرَّاءِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ النُّونِ عَنْهُمَا، كَمَا زَعَمَ المُسْتَشْهِدُ.
His statement: 'al-Murawnaq' with the ra' before the waw and the nun after both, as is commonly known. But the transmitted narration from the author is 'al-Munawraq' with the nun before the waw and the ra' after both. Both have one meaning, which is 'the adorned, the decorated'. However, the second is preferable because it is the transmitted version, and because it is good and sweet due to its rarity and not being common on tongues, unlike the first. Some people sought evidence for the first with the saying of the poet when asked, 'Is the king's handwriting better or the handwriting of Ibn Muqla?' after he saw the two scripts: 'Our master writes the lines of Ibn Muqla and strings them like pearls in a necklace. This one has the adornment of the script alone, and this one has the adornment of the script and the king.' That evidence was refuted because the transmitted narration from the poet is 'nawraq' with the nun before the waw and the ra' after both, not with the ra' before the waw and the nun after both, as the one seeking evidence claimed.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bajuri on the opening line of the matn 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq'. The commentator explains the word 'al-Murawnaq' as commonly vocalized with ra before waw, but notes that the transmitted version from the author is 'al-Munawraq' with nun before waw. Both mean 'adorned' or 'decorated'. He argues for the preference of the second due to its being the actual transmitted version and its pleasing rarity. He then relates a poetic citation used by some to support the first pronunciation, but refutes it by showing that the poet's version also uses 'nawraq' (with nun before waw). The poet's lines compare the king's handwriting to that of the famous calligrapher Ibn Muqla, and the refutation relies on the correct transmitted wording from the poet. The passage thus clarifies a point of philology and textual transmission relevant to the book's title.

Key Terms
مُرَوْنَق
مُنَوْرَق

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) al-Munawnaq
D) al-Murannaq

Key Takeaway
The transmitted version of a term is preferred over the common one, even if the common is widespread, and poetic citations must be checked for exact wording.

Lexical Analysis: Murawnaq vs Munawnaq
Bajuri explains the word 'murawnaq' and its variant with poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two attested pronunciations of the title and evaluate which is preferable based on transmission and poetic evidence.

Core Idea
Textual authenticity takes precedence over common usage in Islamic scholarship.

This lesson examines a seemingly small lexical point: the title of Imam al-Akhdari's famous logic poem, al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. While the common pronunciation is 'murawnaq', the author himself transmitted it as 'munawnaq'. Through this analysis, you will learn how scholars weigh textual transmission against common usage, and how a single line of poetry was used and refuted in this debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pronunciations
The matn title is commonly pronounced 'murawnaq' (ra before waw, nun after). The author's transmission is 'munawnaq' (nun before waw, ra after). Note the word order change.
2
Understand the rationale for preference
Bajuri states that although both mean 'adorned', the second is preferable because it is the transmitted version and its rarity makes it 'pleasant and sweet'. Why does rarity add value?
3
Examine the poetic evidence
Some tried to support 'murawnaq' with a poet's line containing 'rawnaq'. Read the poetry and see how it was supposed to support the common pronunciation.
4
Refute the evidence
Bajuri refutes by showing that the poet actually said 'nawraq' (with nun), not 'rawnaq'. The evidence backfires. Understand the refutation's logic: the transmitted form from the poet matches 'munawnaq' not 'murawnaq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَمَّيْتُهُ بِـ : «السُّلَّمِ المُرَوْنَقِ يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (المُرَوْنَقِ) بِتَقْدِيمِ الرَّاءِ عَلَى الوَاءِ، وَتَأخِيرِ النُّونِ عَنْهُمَا كَمَا اشْتُهِرَ لَكِنَّ المَرْوِيَّ عَنِ المُصَنِّفِ : المُنَورَقِ بِتَقْدِيمِ النُّونِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ الرَّاءِ عَنْهُمَا ، وَهُمَا وَإِنْ كَانَا بِمَعْنًى وَاحِدٍ وَهُوَ : «المُزَيَّنُ المُزَخَّرَفُ، لَكِنْ ثَانِيهَا أَوْلَى؛ لِكَوْنِهِ هُوَ الرِّوَايَةُ، وَلِكَوْنِهِ حَسَناً عَذْباً بِسَبَبٍ غَرَابَتِهِ وَعَدَمِ جَرَيَانِهِ عَلَى الأَلْسِنَةِ، بِخِلَافِ أَوَّلِهِمَا . وَقَدِ اسْتَشْهَدَ بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ بِقَوْلِ الشَّاعِرِ حِينَ سُئِلَ هَلْ خَطُّ المَلِكِ أَحْسَنُ أَوْ خَطُ ابْنِ مُقْلَة؟ بَعْدَ أَنْ رَأَى الخَطَّيْنِ :
يخطّط مَوْلَانَا خُطُوطَ ابْنِ مُقْلة وَيُنَظِمُهَا نَظمَ اللَّآلِي فِي السِّلْكِ فَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَطَّ وَحْدَه وَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَط والمَلِك وَرُدَّ ذَلِكَ الاِسْتِشْهَادُ بِأَنَّ المَرْوِيَّ عَنِ الشَّاعِرِ نَوْرَقُ بِتَقْدِيمِ النُّونِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ الرَّاءِ عَنْهُمَا ، لَا بِتَقْدِيمِ الرَّاءِ عَلَى الوَاوِ وَتَأْخِيرِ النُّونِ عَنْهُمَا، كَمَا زَعَمَ المُسْتَشْهِدُ .
I named it 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' by which the sky of the science of logic is ascended. Bajuri: His statement: (al-Murawnaq) with the ra placed before the waw and the nun after both, as is well-known, but what is transmitted from the author is 'al-Munawnaq' with the nun placed before the waw and the ra after both. Although they both have the same meaning—'the adorned, the ornamented'—the second is preferable because it is the transmission and because it is pleasant and sweet due to its rarity and not being common on tongues, unlike the first. Some have sought evidence for the first with the poet's statement when asked: 'Is the king's handwriting better or that of Ibn Muqlah?' after seeing the two lines: 'Our lord writes the lines of Ibn Muqlah and strings them like pearls in a necklace. This one has the splendor (rawnaq) of the line alone, and this one has the splendor of the line and the king.' But this evidence was refuted by noting that what is transmitted from the poet is 'nawraq' with the nun before the waw and the ra after both, not with the ra before the waw and the nun after both, as the seeker of evidence claimed.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and philological commentary on the word 'murawnaq' in the title of the matn. The commentator (Bajuri) first clarifies the common pronunciation: with ra before waw and nun after. Then he notes that the authentic transmission from the author (the matn writer) is 'munawnaq'—with nun before waw and ra after. Although both mean 'adorned' or 'ornamented', the second is better because it is the transmitted version and because its rarity makes it sweet. He then relates an attempt by some to prove the first pronunciation using a line of poetry about a king's calligraphy. The poet supposedly said 'rawnaq' (splendor) but the transmitted version is 'nawraq'. Thus the evidence is refuted. The lesson shows care in textual transmission and lexical preference in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Key Terms
مُرَوْنَق
مُنَوْرَق
رَوْنَق

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Murawnaq
B) Munawnaq
C) Rawnaq
D) Nawraq

Key Takeaway
In Islamic textual analysis, the author's own transmission and rare forms often carry more weight than common usage, especially when supported by correct poetic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Sky Metaphor in al-Sullam al-Murawnaq
Bajuri explains the metaphor and its rhetorical variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the rhetorical metaphor types (tashrihiyya and makniyya) used in the title of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq.

Core Idea
The phrase 'sky of logic' is a layered metaphor with multiple valid interpretations.

Bajuri unpacks the metaphor 'sky of logic' in the opening line of al-Sullam. He explains that the idafa construction presents logic as something similar to the sky in loftiness. Then he explores two possible rhetorical analyses: an explicit metaphor likening difficult issues to the sky, or an implicit metaphor likening logic to a star, with 'sky' as a concomitant. He also notes that the word 'ascends' reinforces the metaphor (tarshih). This lesson shows how careful rhetorical analysis can reveal the depth of a single phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphor phrase
Find the phrase 'سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ' in the Arabic. Note that 'سماء' (sky) is a metaphor for something lofty.
2
Analyze the idafa construction
Bajuri says this is 'إِضَافَةُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّه' meaning the likened-to is added to the likened. Here, 'sky' (the likened-to) is added to 'science of logic' (the likened), indicating that logic is like the sky in loftiness.
This is a common rhetorical device.
3
Distinguish the two metaphor types
Bajuri presents two possibilities: (a) Explicit metaphor (tashrihiyya): the difficult issues of logic are likened to the sky, borrowing the name 'sky' for them. (b) Implicit metaphor (makniyya): logic itself is likened to a star (guidance), and 'sky' is mentioned as a concomitant of the star.
The second type omits the likened-to and mentions something associated with it.
4
Recognize the tarshih (reinforcement)
The word 'يُرْقَى بِهِ' (by which one ascends) fits both interpretations and reinforces the metaphor by mentioning an action appropriate to the sky.
Tarshih makes the metaphor more vivid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَمَّيْتُهُ بِـ : السُّلَّمِ المُرَوْنَقِ يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءَ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ وَالصَّمِيرُ يَرْجِعُ لِلمُؤَلِّفِ الَّذِي رَجَعَ إِلَيْهِ الضَّمِيرُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : سَمَّيْتُهُ ، وَكَذَلِكَ الضَّمَائِرُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَأَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصاً ... إلخ»، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنَ الشَّرْحِ الصَّغِيرِ» لِلشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ (١) وَيَصِحُ رُجُوعُ ذَلِكَ لِـ «السُّلَّمِ المُتَقَدِّمِ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ فِي الشَّرْحِ الكَبِيرِ) (۲) ، لَكِنْ يَتَعَيَّنُ أَنْ يُرَادَ بِهِ (٢)، المُسَمَّى لَا الاِسْمُ ، كَمَا هُوَ المُرَادُ بِهِ (۳) فِيمَا سَبَقَ ، فَيَكُونُ فِيهِ اسْتِخْدَامُ، لَكِنَّ الأَوَّلَ أَوْلَى كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى . وَقَوْلُهُ : (سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ) أَيْ : عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ الشَّبِيهِ بِالسَّمَاءِ فِي العُلُوِّ ، فَإِضَافَةُ «سَمَاءِ» لِمَا بَعْدَهُ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّه . لا يُقَالُ : يَلْزَمُ عَلَى كَلَام المُصَنِّفِ تَوْصِيلُ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ هَذَا المُؤَلَّفَ بَعْضُ المَنْطِقِ، وَقَدْ جَعَلَهُ مُوصلاً لِعِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ، المُشْتَمِل عَلَى ذَلِكَ البَعْضِ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ هَذَا المُؤَلَّفَ أَلْفَاظٌ لَا مَعَانٍ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مَا ذُكِرَ، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيم أَنَّهُ مَعَانٍ فَالمُرَادُ : أَنَّهُ يُرْقَى بِهِ لِمَا عَدَاهُ مِنْ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ، لَا لِجَمِيعِهِ الشَّامِلِ لَهُ. هَذَا؛ وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِي كَلَامِهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مَكْنِيَّةٌ : فَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ : يَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ المَسَائِلَ الصَّعْبَةَ مِنْ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ بِمَعْنَى : السَّمَاءِ؛ بِجَامِعِ : عُسْرِ التَنَاوِلِ فِي كُلِّ، وَاسْتَعَارَ اسْمَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ . وَعَلَى الثَّانِيَةِ : يَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ عِلْمَ المَنْطِقِ بِالنَّجْمِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الاهْتِدَاءِ بِكُلِّ تَشْبِيها مُضْمَراً فِي النَّفْسِ، وَحَذَفَ اسْمَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَأَثْبَتَ لَهُ شَيْئاً مِنْ لَوَازِمِهِ وَهُوَ السَّمَاءُ؛ إِمَّا بَاقِياً عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ الحَقِيقِيِّ، أَوْ مُسْتَعَاراً لِلمَسَائِلِ الصَّعْبَةِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْأَوْجُهِ الأَرْبَعَةِ) يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «يُرْقَى بِهِ» تَرْشِيحاً ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ .
I named it 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq' by which one ascends to the sky of the science of logic. The pronoun refers to the author to whom the pronoun in his saying 'I named it' refers, and likewise the pronouns in his saying 'and that it be pure...' etc., as taken from al-Mallawi's al-Sharh al-Saghir. And it is valid for that to refer to the preceding 'al-Sullam', as he mentioned in al-Sharh al-Kabir, but it must be intended by it the named entity, not the name, just as it was intended in what preceded, so there is usage in it, but the first is more appropriate, as is not hidden. And his saying 'sky of the science of logic' means: the science of logic similar to the sky in loftiness, so the idafa of 'sky' to what follows is idafa of the likened-to to the likened. It cannot be said: a necessary consequence of the author's speech is that a thing leads to itself, because this work is part of logic, and he made it a means to the science of logic which includes that part. For we say: it is not hidden that this work is words, not meanings, so the mentioned consequence does not follow; and if it is granted that it is meanings, then the intended meaning is that by it one ascends to the rest of the science of logic, not to all of it including it. [Then after a note:] This, and it is valid that in his speech there is either tashrihiyya or makniyya metaphor. On the first: he likened the difficult issues of logic to the sky, with the commonality of difficulty of attainment, and borrowed the name of the likened-to for the likened. On the second: he likened the science of logic to a star, with the commonality of guidance, a simile implied in the mind, and he omitted the name of the likened-to and established for it one of its concomitants, which is 'sky', either remaining on its literal meaning or borrowed for the difficult issues. On each of these four aspects, his saying 'by which one ascends' is a tarshih, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the first line of the Sullam al-Murawnaq. Bajuri begins by clarifying the pronouns: 'I named it' refers to the author, though some say it could refer to the poem itself. He argues it is better to take the pronoun as referring to the author. Then he explains the phrase 'sky of the science of logic' as a metaphor where logic is compared to the sky in loftiness, and the idafa is of the likened-to to the likened (i.e., logic is like the sky). He then anticipates an objection: if this very book is part of logic, how can it be a ladder to logic itself? That would be a thing leading to itself, which is invalid. Bajuri responds with two arguments: first, that the book is letters/words, not meanings, so it is not part of the science; second, even if it were meanings, it leads to the rest of logic, not to its entirety including itself. Finally, he provides a deeper rhetorical analysis, suggesting two possibilities: either the metaphor is tashrihiyya (explicit, likening difficult issues to the sky) or makniyya (implicit, likening logic to a star, with 'sky' as a concomitant). On the second, the word 'sky' can be literal or metaphorical. In all cases, 'yurqā bihi' is a tarshih (reinforcement) of the metaphor. This shows the precision of the author's poetic expression.

Key Terms
السُّلَّم المُرَوْنَق
سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ
إِضَافَةُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّه
اسْتِعَارَة تَصْرِيحِيَّة
اسْتِعَارَة مَكْنِيَّة
تَرْشِيح

Key Takeaway
The metaphor in 'sky of logic' is deliberately crafted to allow multiple valid rhetorical interpretations, each enriched by tarshih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Defending the Ladder from Self-Reference
Bajuri defends the metaphor against a logical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Bajuri's defense against the charge that the poem leads to itself, and evaluate the strength of his two arguments.

Core Idea
A part of a science can still lead to the whole science without circularity.

An objection is raised: if the poem 'al-Sullam' is itself part of logic, how can it be a ladder to logic? That would be a thing leading to itself, which is absurd. Bajuri gives two responses: first, the poem consists of words (alfaz), not meanings (ma'ani), so it is not part of the science; second, even if it were meanings, it leads to the rest of logic, not its entirety. This lesson walks through the objection and response logically, showing how the issue of self-reference is resolved in Islamic commentary tradition.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector says: If the poem is part of logic, then using it to reach logic is like a thing leading to itself. This is a violation of logical consistency. Write the objection in your own words.
The objection is based on the principle that a part cannot lead to the whole that includes it in a simple causal way.
2
Examine Bajuri's first response
Bajuri argues that the poem is 'alfaz la ma'ani' (words, not meanings). As a physical text, it is not part of the conceptual science of logic. Therefore, the objection collapses because the poem is not intrinsically part of logic.
This distinguishes between the physical artifact and the abstract science.
3
Examine Bajuri's second response
Even if we grant that the poem contains meanings and is part of logic, Bajuri says the intended meaning is that it leads to 'what remains' of logic (ma 'adahu), not to all of logic including itself. So the leading is to the rest, not to itself.
This is a further conditional defense, accepting the premise but refining the conclusion.
4
Evaluate the strengths and weaknesses
Consider whether Bajuri's first response is sufficient. Does distinguishing words from meanings truly avoid the problem? What if a book of logic is considered part of the science? The second response seems more robust: the ladder leads to other parts, not to itself.
In the Islamic tradition, this kind of careful distinction is common in usul and logic.
