Aristotle and Al-Farabi: Founders of Islamic Logic
Author discusses the name, epithets, and biography of Aristotle, and the role of Al-Farabi in transmitting logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the roles of Aristotle and Al-Farabi in the origin and transmission of logic to Islamic civilization, and evaluate the Islamic stance on logic as a foreign science.

Core Idea
Aristotle founded logic; Al-Farabi compiled it in Islam; together they show that logic is not alien to revelation.

This lesson explores the biographical details of Aristotle, the founder of logic, and Al-Farabi, the first to compile logic in Islam. You will learn why Aristotle is called the 'Greatest Teacher', how he received patronage from Dhul Qarnayn, and how Al-Farabi's encounter with Sayf al-Dawla demonstrated his mastery. The passage also addresses the concern that logic is a foreign science, arguing that it was adopted after Islam and is compatible with divine law.

Guided Path
1
Identify Aristotle's Names and Title
The passage gives three Arabic names for Aristotle: Arisṭū, Rūsṭālīs, and al-Mu'allim al-Akbar. Note the variations and the reason (non-Arabic names undergo change).
Compare with Jibril and Ibrahim variations.
2
Understand Aristotle's Role in Founding Logic
He is called the 'Greatest Teacher' because he founded logical teachings and brought them from potentiality to actuality. This occurred under the patronage of Dhul Qarnayn the Roman.
Consider the significance of this patronage for the legitimacy of logic.
3
Extract the Wisdom Lineage and the Quote from Politics
Aristotle took wisdom from Plato, who took it from Socrates. The quote 'Whoever scorns the law, the law kills him' indicates he believed in divine law, countering the idea that logic is purely pagan.
This quote is used to show that logic is not inherently un-Islamic.
4
Analyze Al-Farabi's Story at the Court of Sayf al-Dawla
The story illustrates Al-Farabi's linguistic prowess (over 70 languages), his scholarly dominance (silencing all scholars), and his ability to use music to control emotions. This shows the ideal of a complete sage.
Note how Al-Farabi's actions mirror the goal of logic: to bring order and clarity.
5
Reflect on the Islamic Reception of Logic
The author addresses the objection that logic is from the science of disbelievers by explaining that it was taken after Islam from their hands, analogous to how other sciences were adopted.
This legitimizes the study of logic within Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أَرِسْطُو) ويقال له أيضاً : رُسطاليس» - بضم الراء وحذف الهمزة من أوَّله وإحدى الطائين ، وقد وقع في قول أبي الطيب : [من الكامل] مَنْ مُبْلِعُ الأَعْرَابِ أَنِّي بَعْدَهَا جَالَسْتُ رُسُطَالِيسَ وَالإسْكَندَرًا . وهو عجميّ، والأسماء العجميَّة كثيراً ما يعتريها التَّغيير ؛ كما قالوا : «جبريل، وجبرائيل، وجبرين»، و«إبراهيم، وإبراهام - بالألف » . ويقال له: «المعلم الأكبر»؛ لأنَّه واضعُ التَّعاليم المنطقيَّة ومخرجها مِنَ القوَّة إلى الفعل، وكان ذلك في زمن ذي القرنين الرُّوميّ ؛ قيل : إنَّه بذل له خمس مئة ألف دينار، ورتَّب له كل سنة مئة ألف وعشرين ألف دينار. وكان أرسطو كبير حكماء الخليقة غير منازع ، أخذ الحكمة عن أفلاطون، وهو عن سقراط، وكان مسكنه مدينة أثينا، قتله قومه مسموماً لمَّا نهاهم عن عبادة الأوثان، ومن كلامه في كتاب «السياسة» : مَنِ استخف بالناموس قتله النَّاموس أي : مَنِ استخفَّ بالشَّرع قتله الشَّرع، وهذا يدلُّ على أنَّه كان مؤمناً بشرع زمانه فقولهم : هذا العلم من علم الكفرة ؛ معناه : أخذ بعد الإسلام من أيديهم. وأوَّل مَن دونه في الإسلام - كما قال الغزالي - الإمام أبو نصر محمد بن محمد الفارابي التركي الحكيم المشهور، كان أعجوبة الزَّمان ارتحل من بلده فاراب إلى بغداد وكان لا يجارى في علم المنطق وجميع علوم الفلسفة، وذكر ابن خلكان : أنَّه لما ورد على سيف الدولة، وكان مجلسه مجلس الفضلاء، فدخل عليهم في زَيّ الأتراك، وكان ذلك دأبه، فقال له سيف الدولة: «اقعد»، قال: حيث أنا أم حيث أنت؟»، فقال: حيث أنت»، وتخطّى رقاب النَّاس إلى مجلس سيف الدَّولة، وزاحمه فيه حتّى أخرجه عنه، فقال الأمير لمملوك على رأسه بلسان قلَّ مَن يعرفُهُ : «إنَّ الشَّيخ أساء الأدب، وإني مسائله عن أشياء، فإن لم يُجب فاخرقوا به»، فقال له أبو نصر بذلك اللسان: اصبر، فإنَّ الأمور بعواقبها، فتعجب الأمير وقال له: «أتحسن هذا اللسان؟»، قال: «أحسن أكثر من سبعين لساناً»، فعظم عنده . وأخذ يتكلم مع العلماء في كل فن حتَّى أسكت الكل، وبقي يتكلم وحده وهم يكتبون عنه، فأخرجهم الأمير وخلا به فقال له : هل لك في أن تأكل؟»، قال: «لا». فقال: «أتشرب؟»، قال: «لا». قال: «أتسمع؟»، قال: «نعم»، فاحضر القينان وأنواع الملاهي، فما حرَّك أحد منهم إلا عابه فقال له الأمير: «أتحسن شيئاً من هذا؟ قال: «نعم»، ثمَّ أخرج خريطة فيها عيدان فركَّبها، ولعب بها، فضحك كل من في المجلس حتَّى البواب، ثمَّ حرَّكها ثانياً فبكوا، ثم ثالثاً فناموا، فتركهم نياماً وخرج (۱) ، توفي سنة ٣٣٩ هجرية ودفن بظاهر دمشق
His saying: 'Aristo' – and it is also said: 'Rustalis' – with a damma on the ra' and dropping the hamza from its beginning and one of the two tā's. It occurs in the saying of Abu al-Tayyib: [the verse]. And it is non-Arabic, and non-Arabic names often undergo change, as they said: 'Jibril, Jibra'il, Jibrin' and 'Ibrahim, Ibraham' with an alif. He is called 'the Greatest Teacher' because he is the founder of logical teachings and the one who brought them from potentiality to actuality. That was in the time of Dhul Qarnayn the Roman; it is said that he gave him five hundred thousand dinars and arranged for him an annual salary of one hundred and twenty thousand dinars. Aristotle was the greatest of the wise men of creation, unsurpassed. He took wisdom from Plato, who took it from Socrates. His residence was the city of Athens. His people killed him by poison when he forbade them from idol worship. Among his sayings in the book 'Politics': 'Whoever scorns the law, the law kills him', i.e., whoever scorns the Shari'a, the Shari'a kills him. This indicates that he was a believer in the Shari'a of his time. So their saying that this science is from the science of the disbelievers means it was taken after Islam from their hands. The first to compile it in Islam – as al-Ghazali said – was Imam Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Farabi al-Turki, the famous sage. He was the wonder of his time. He traveled from his town Farab to Baghdad, and he was unmatched in the science of logic and all philosophical sciences. Ibn Khallikan mentioned that when he came to Sayf al-Dawla, whose gathering was a gathering of learned men, he entered among them in the garb of the Turks, which was his habit. Sayf al-Dawla said to him, 'Sit.' He said, 'Where I am or where you are?' He said, 'Where you are.' So he stepped over the necks of the people to the assembly of Sayf al-Dawla and crowded him out of his place. The prince said to a servant at his head in a language that few knew: 'The sheikh has been ill-mannered. I will ask him about things; if he does not answer, then tear him apart.' Abu Nasr said in that same language: 'Be patient, for matters depend on their outcomes.' The prince was amazed and said to him, 'Do you know this language?' He said, 'I know more than seventy languages.' So he became great in his esteem. He began to speak with the scholars in every field until he silenced everyone. He continued speaking alone while they wrote from him. The prince dismissed them and stayed alone with him and said, 'Would you like to eat?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Would you like to drink?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Will you listen?' He said, 'Yes.' So he brought singing girls and various types of entertainment. Not a single one of them moved except that he criticized them. The prince said to him, 'Do you know anything of this?' He said, 'Yes.' Then he took out a pouch containing sticks, assembled them, and played with them. Everyone in the gathering laughed, including the doorkeeper. Then he shook them a second time and they all wept. Then a third time and they all slept. He left them sleeping and left. He died in the year 339 AH and was buried in the outskirts of Damascus.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and historical note on the founder of logic, Aristotle, and the first Muslim logician, Al-Farabi. The author clarifies the different names for Aristotle, his title 'al-Mu'allim al-Akbar', his role in bringing logic from potentiality to actuality, his patronage by Dhul Qarnayn, his lineage of wisdom from Plato and Socrates, his martyrdom by poison, and a quote from his book Politics indicating his belief in divine law. Then he addresses the issue of logic being viewed as a science of the disbelievers, explaining that it was taken from them after Islam. He then narrates the story of Al-Farabi, his arrival at the court of Sayf al-Dawla, his linguistic prowess, his scholarly dominance, and his musical trick, concluding with his death date.

Key Terms
أرسطو
المعلم الأكبر
رُسطاليس
الفارابي
سيف الدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is Aristotle called 'al-Mu'allim al-Akbar' according to this passage?

Key Takeaway
Logic was founded by Aristotle, respected by him as a divine science, and transmitted to Islam by Al-Farabi, who embodied the ideal of the complete sage.

The Virtue of the Science of Logic
Author explains that the virtue of a science is measured by its benefit, and logic's virtue is that it judges other sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relationship between the benefit and virtue of a science, and state why logic's virtue is its role as arbiter over other sciences.

Core Idea
Logic's virtue is its role as judge over all other sciences.

This opening passage introduces the virtue (fadila) of logic. The author explains that a science's virtue is tied to its benefit. Logic's unique benefit is its ability to evaluate reasoning across disciplines, making it the arbiter over other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify key concepts
Find the terms فضيلة (virtue) and فائدة (benefit) in the Arabic. How does the author define their relationship?
2
Understand the motivation
The commentary says scholars mentioned virtue separately from benefit to motivate students. Why is this extra emphasis important for learning logic?
3
Extract logic's specific virtue
The passage says logic is 'الحاكم على ما سواه' (the arbiter over other sciences). What does this mean concretely?
Think of logic as a quality control tool for arguments in any field.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Give an example: In usul al-fiqh, logic evaluates analogical arguments (qiyas) to ensure they follow valid reasoning patterns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما فضيلته، فاعلم أن فضيلة كل علم بقدر شرف فائدته، فبمعرفة فائدة العلم تعرف فضيلته. قوله : (وأما فضيلته) إنما لم يكتفوا بمعرفة الفائدة عن معرفة الفضيلة؛ تنشيطاً للطالب، وزيادةً في رغبته وناهيك بفضيلة هذا العلم أنه : الحاكم على ما سواه بالرد والقبول.
As for its virtue, know that the virtue of every science is in accordance with the honor of its benefit, so by knowing the benefit of a science its virtue is known. His saying: (As for its virtue) — they did not content themselves with knowing the benefit instead of knowing the virtue, in order to motivate the student and increase his desire. And sufficient for you regarding the virtue of this science is that it is the arbiter over other sciences, accepting and rejecting.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage discusses the fadila (virtue) of the science of logic. The matn states that the virtue of any science is proportional to the honor of its benefit. Therefore, knowing the benefit of a science reveals its virtue. The commentary adds that scholars did not rely solely on benefit to indicate virtue; they explicitly mention virtue to motivate students and increase their desire. The crowning virtue of logic is that it acts as the hakim (arbiter) over other sciences, judging their claims by accepting or rejecting them. This highlights logic's unique role as the meta-science that evaluates reasoning in all fields. All examples are implicit: logic rules over other Islamic sciences like fiqh, kalam, etc.

Key Takeaway
The virtue of a science is proportional to the honor of its benefit, and logic's supreme benefit is its role as the judge over all other sciences.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Benefit of Logic: Grammar for the Mind
Clarification of the analogy between logic and grammar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the benefit of logic by comparing it to the role of grammar, and refute a common misunderstanding about the analogy.

Core Idea
Logic protects the mind from error just as grammar protects speech.

When the author of al-Sullam says logic's relationship is like grammar to the tongue, he is not stating a technical definition. He is pointing to its benefit (fa'ida). In this lesson, you will unpack this analogy and see why logic is so valuable for the pursuit of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
The author says logic's relationship (nisba) to the intellect is like grammar to the tongue. What two things are being compared?
2
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explicitly says the author did not mean the technical relationship (nisba istilahiyya) but the benefit (fa'ida). Why does he say this? Look at the evidence he provides.
He cites the author's later words: 'for the heart' and 'for it protects' as proof.
3
Extract the benefit statement
The commentator states: 'the benefit of logic is the protection of the mind from error in sciences' (fa'idat al-mantiq siyanat al-dhihn 'an al-khata' fiha). What does this mean?
4
Apply the analogy
Just as grammar protects the tongue from error in speech, logic protects the mind from error in reasoning. How does this help you personally in your studies?
Consider how logic safeguards your understanding in any Islamic science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُ النَّاظِمِ : (نِسْبَتُهُ كَالنَّحْوِ لِلْسَانِ) لَمْ يُرِدْ بِهِ بَيَانَ النِّسْبَةِ الاِصْطِلاحِيَّةِ، بَلِ المُرَادُ بِهَا : الفَائِدَةُ ; بِدَلِيلِ قَوْلِهِ : لِلْجَنَانِ»، وَقَوْلِهِ : «فَيَعْصِمُ ... إلخ»، خِلَافاً لِمَنْ وَهِمَ. وَإِذَا عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ إِنَّمَا فُضْلَ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ المُدْرِكَةِ لِلعُلُومِ، وَعَلِمْتَ أَنَّ فَائِدَةَ المَنْطِقِ صِيَانَةُ الذِّهْنِ عَنِ الخَطَأ فِيهَا ، عَلِمْتَ شُفُوفَ هَذَا العِلْمِ وَفَضْلِهِ وَعُلُوَّ دَرَجَتِهِ، وَمِمَّا يُنْسَبُ لِلْغَزَالِيِّ فِي مَدْحِهِ:
حِكْمَةُ المَنْطِقِ شَيْءٌ عَجَـيبُ وَاخْتِلَافُ النَّاسِ فِيهِ أَعْجَبُ
كُلُّ عِلْمٍ هُوَقَانُونُ لَهُ وَبِهِ يُدْرَكُ مَا يُسْتَصْعَبُ
وَلَهُ فِي نَفْسٍ مَنْ لَمْ يَرَهُ نَفْرَةٌ تُوجِبُ مَا لَا يَجِبُ
أَدَبُ عَـنْ لَـــديـــــهِ أَدَبُ
وَكَذَا يَنْــرُ مَنْ لَيْسَ لَهُ
وَمِنْ حَيْثُ إنَّها باعثةٌ للفاعل على الإقدام على الفعل ; تسمَّى: «عِلَّةٌ غائيَّةٌ». فالفائدة والغاية متحدان بالذات مختلفان بالاعتبار، كما أنَّ الغَرَض والعِلَّة الغائيَّة كذلك . فأفعال الله تعالى يترتب عليها حِكَمٌ ومصالح لا تحصى، فذهب الأشاعرة والحكماء إلى أن تلك المصالح غايات لأفعاله تعالى ومنافعها راجعةٌ إلى المخلوقات وليس شيء منها غرضاً له وعِلَّةٌ غائيَّةٌ لفعله ; لأنَّ الغرض يؤدّي إلى نُقصانه سبحانه تعالى، وهو محال، وما ورد من الأحاديث والآيات الموهمة لكون أفعاله تعالى معللة بالأغراض، فهي محمولةٌ على الغايات المرتبة عليها، وليس شيءٌ مِن أفعاله خالياً عن المصلحة .
قوله : (شُفُوفَ) أي : رقة ولطافة هذا العلم.
قوله : (وَمِمَّا يُنْسَبُ لِلْغَزَالِي فِي مَدْحِهِ) الغزالي : هو أبو عبد الله محمد بن محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي، العلامة الماهر الرَّئيس في العلوم كلها، الجامع بين الشريعة والحقيقة، يلقب: حجة الإسلام»، كان أبوه غزالاً فاشتهر بالغزال، فنُسِب إليه، كان من أئمة الشَّافعيَّة، له تأليف مشهورةٌ في الأصول والفقه وعلم الدين، توفي تكاته سنة خمس وخمس مئة .
ولبعضهم أيضاً في مدح المنطق :
كَمُلْ حَقِيقَةُ مَنْطِقٍ بِمَنْطِقِهِمْ أَكْرِمْ بِتَحْقِيقِ عِلْمٍ مِنْ قَوَاعِدِهِ
And the author's statement: (Its relationship is like grammar to the tongue). He did not intend by it an explanation of the technical relationship, rather the intended meaning by it is the benefit, as evidence for his statement: 'for the heart' and his statement: 'for it protects... etc.', contrary to whoever is mistaken. And when you know that man was only distinguished over other animals by the rational faculty that perceives sciences, and you know that the benefit of logic is the protection of the mind from error in them, you know the subtlety of this science, its virtue, and the loftiness of its rank. And among what is attributed to al-Ghazali in praise of it: 'The wisdom of logic is a wondrous thing, and people's disagreement about it is more wondrous. Every science is a canon for it, and by it what is difficult is grasped. And in the soul of one who has not seen it, there is aversion that necessitates what is not necessary. Etiquette from him is etiquette.' And likewise one who has no etiquette... And insofar as it is a motive for the agent to undertake the action, it is called 'final cause'. So benefit and end are united in essence, different in consideration, as is the case with purpose and final cause. Thus upon the actions of Allah the Exalted follow wisdoms and benefits beyond count. The Ash'aris and philosophers held that those benefits are ends for His actions, and their benefits return to the creation, and none of them is a purpose for Him or a final cause for His action, because purpose leads to deficiency in Him, Exalted, and that is impossible. And what has come of hadiths and verses that suggest that His actions are caused by purposes are interpreted as the ends that are arranged upon them, and none of His actions is devoid of benefit.
His statement: (shufuf) i.e., the delicacy and subtlety of this science.
His statement: (And among what is attributed to al-Ghazali in praise of him.) Al-Ghazali: He is Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi, the skilled master in all sciences, the unifier of Sharia and reality, titled the Proof of Islam. His father was a spinner, so he became known as al-Ghazali, attributed to him. He was among the imams of the Shafi'i school. He has famous works in usul, fiqh, and religious sciences. He died in Tus in the year 505.
And by some others also in praise of logic:
'The reality of logic has been perfected by their logic, honor the realization of a science from its principles.'
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq where the author says 'its relationship is like grammar to the tongue'. The commentator clarifies that the author does not intend the technical relationship but rather the benefit: just as grammar protects speech from error, logic protects the mind from error in acquiring knowledge. He refutes a mistaken interpretation. Then he explains the virtue of logic: since man is distinguished by the rational faculty that perceives sciences, and logic safeguards that faculty, its rank is high. He then quotes two poems in praise of logic, one attributed to al-Ghazali and another anonymous. The first poem highlights the wondrous nature of logic and that it is the canon for all sciences. Then the commentator digresses to discuss the difference between benefit (fa'ida) and final cause ('illa gha'iyya), noting they are the same in essence but differ in consideration. He applies this to divine actions: the benefits of Allah's actions return to creation, and He is not motivated by purposes, which would imply deficiency. The passage ends with a brief biography of al-Ghazali.

Key Terms
فائدة
نسبة اصطلاحية
صيانة الذهن

C) the subject matter of logic
D) the virtue of logic

Key Takeaway
Logic is the guardian of the mind, ensuring our reasoning is sound across all sciences.

Introduction and Front Matter

Benefit vs. Final Cause: A Theological Distinction
Distinction between fa'ida and 'illa gha'iyya in divine actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between benefit (fa'ida) and final cause ('illa gha'iyya) and apply this distinction to divine actions in the Ash'ari tradition.

Core Idea
Allah's actions have benefits for creation but are not caused by purposes.

The commentator expands on the notion of benefit by introducing the concept of final cause. He clarifies that benefit and final cause are the same in essence but differ in consideration. More importantly, he applies this to Allah's actions: they are full of wisdom and benefit, but attributing a purpose to Allah would imply deficiency. This lesson unpacks that subtle theological point and its connection to the virtue of logic.

Guided Path
1
Define benefit and final cause
Benefit (fa'ida) is the good that results; final cause ('illa gha'iyya) is the motive for action. The commentator says they are united in essence but different in consideration. What does that mean?
They are the same thing viewed from different angles.
2
Identify the Asha'ri position
The Ash'aris and philosophers hold that Allah's actions have benefits that return to creation, but He is not motivated by purposes. Why would attributing a purpose be problematic?
It implies need or deficiency in Allah, which is impossible.
3
Understand the interpretation of ambiguous texts
Verses and hadith that seem to attribute purposes to Allah are to be interpreted as ends (ghayat) that result from His actions, not as motivations.
4
Connect to the virtue of logic
Logic helps us make such distinctions precisely, avoiding theological errors.
This shows the benefit of logic extends to theology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Understanding Key Terms in the Opening of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq
Commentary defines 'haka', 'usul', 'funun', and 'murawnaq'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical and lexical meanings of the terms 'haka', 'usul', 'funun', and 'murawnaq' as used in the opening of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq, and explain their significance for studying logic.

Core Idea
Precise definition of opening terms frames logic as a tool for extracting knowledge from foundational rules.

The opening line of any classical text sets the tone for the entire work. In al-Sullam al-Murawnaq, the poet uses carefully chosen words: 'haka' (take), 'usul' (foundations), 'funun' (branches), and 'murawnaq' (adorned). This lesson unpacks each term to reveal the pedagogical and epistemological commitments of the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical category of 'haka'
Notice that 'haka' is an action noun (ism fi'l) meaning 'take', not an imperative verb. The commentator gives its conjugations for gender and number: haaka, haaki, haakumaa, haakum, haakunna. This grammatical precision is typical of scholarly definitions.
The 'kaf' is a letter of address, and can be replaced with a hamza (haa).
2
Analyze 'min usulihi' (from its foundations)
The partitive 'min' indicates taking some from a set. 'Usul' (singular: asl) means foundations or rules—here, the basic principles of logic. This term is used in many Islamic sciences, but here it refers to logical axioms.
Compare to 'usul al-fiqh' – the foundations of jurisprudence.
3
Interpret 'fununihi' (its branches)
'Funun' (singular: fann) means branches or details. The commentator says they are the 'particulars of those rules' – i.e., the specific conclusions derived from the usul. This shows that logic has a hierarchical structure.
In other contexts, 'funun' can mean arts or sciences.
4
Decode 'al-murawnaq' (the adorned)
Derived from 'rawnaq' meaning luster or beauty. The commentator cites lexical evidence and poetry to confirm it means 'beautified'. The title 'The Adorned Ladder' suggests that this text beautifies the ascent to logical knowledge.
The commentator also mentions an alternative: 'al-muwaththaq' (the strengthened), but prefers 'al-murawnaq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۱۱) فَهَاكَ مِنْ أُصُولِهِ قَواعِدًا تَجْمَعُ مِنْ فُنُونِهِ فَوائِدًا (۱۲) سَمَّيْتُهُ بِـ: «السُّلَّمِ المُرَوْنَقِ يُرْقَى بِهِ سَمَاءُ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ «هَاكَ»: اسْمُ فِعْلٍ بِمَعْنَى: «خُذْ»، و«الكاف»: حَرْفُ خِطَاب ؛ يُفْتَحُ لِلْمُذَكَّرِ وَيُكْسَرُ لِلْمُؤنَّثِ، وَيُثَنَّى وَيُجْمَعُ ؛ فَيُقَالُ : هَاكَ، وَهَاكِ، وَهَاكُمَا ، وَهَاكُمْ، وَهَاكُنَّ»، وَقَدْ تُبْدَلُ مِنَ الكَافِ هَمْزَةٌ ، تَتَصَرَّفُ (۱) كَتَصَرُّفِهِ فَيُقَالُ: «هَاءَ» لِلْمُذَكَّرِ بِفَتْح الهَمْ بِفَتْح الهَمْزَةِ، وَ«هَاءِ» لِلْمُؤَنَّثِ بِكَسْرِهَا ، وَاهَاؤمَا، وَهَاؤمَ، وَهَاؤنَّ»، وَبِهَذِهِ اللُّغَةِ جَاءَ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿ هَاؤُمُ اقْرَءُوا كِنَبِيَّة [الحاقة : ١٩]؛ أَيْ: هَاكُمْ. قَلِيلَةٌ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ أُصُولِهِ) أَيْ: مِنْ قَوَاعِدِهِ، وَمِنْ تَبْعِيضِيَّةٌ ، أَيْ: خُذْ مِنْ قَوَاعِدِهِ قَوَاعِدَ وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «فُنُونِهِ : جُزْيَّاتُ تِلْكَ القَوَاعِدِ . وَالمُرَوْنَقُ» : المُحَسَنُ ؛ فِي الصَّحَاحِ»: «رَوْنَقُ السَّيْفِ : مَاؤُهُ وحُسْنُهُ » (۲) . ؛ قصارة. فَكُلُّ عِلْمٍ رَقِيقٍ عِلْمُ مَقْصِدِهِ وَكُلُّ فَهْــم دَقِـيـق مِنْ فَوَائِدِهِ قوله : (وَمِنْ) تَبعِيضيَّةٌ) الظاهر أنَّها للبيان فيهما، كما عند عبد اللطيف قوله : (المُرَوْنَقُ) أَوْلى منه: «الموثق)) وأنسب. والمرونق المزيَّن أنشد النَّاظم في «شرحه» قول الشاعر : فَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخَط وحده وَهَذَا عَلَيْهِ رَوْنَقُ الخط والمَلِك ولا بن سهل في المعنى ـ وما ألطفه - : [من الكامل] قَدْ جَرَى مَاءُ النَّعِيمِ بِوَجْهِهِ فَاسْوَدَّ مَجْرَى المَاءِ فِي الجَمَرَاتِ كَتَبَتْ حُرُوفَ الشِّعْرِ فِي وَجَنَاتِهِ مَا قَدْ جَنَتْ عَيْنَاهُ فِي المُهجَاتِ فَتَرَى ذُنُوبَ جُفُونِهِ فِي خَدِّهِ لَكِنْ عَلَيْهَا رَوْنَقُ الحَسَنَاتِ
His statement: (11) 'So take from its foundations rules that gather from its branches benefits.' (12) 'I named it 'The Adorned Ladder by which the sky of the science of logic is ascended.' 'Haaka' is an action noun meaning 'take'; the 'kaf' is a letter of address; it is opened for masculine and broken for feminine, dual and plural. So it is said: 'haaka, haaki, haakumaa, haakum, haakunna'. And sometimes the 'kaf' is replaced with a hamza, which conjugates like it; so it is said: 'haa' for masculine with fatha on the hamza, and 'haa'i' for feminine with kasra, and 'haa'umaa, haa'um, haa'unna'. And with this language came the statement of Allah the Exalted: 'Here, read my book!' [69:19]; meaning: 'take it'. (And his statement: 'min usulihi') meaning: from its rules, and 'min' is partitive, i.e., 'take from its rules rules.' And what is meant by 'funuunihi' is the particulars of those rules. And 'al-murawnaq' means the beautified; in 'al-Sihah': 'rawnaq al-sayf: its water and beauty'. (He said: 'wa min' partitive) apparently it is for clarification in both, as with 'Abd al-Latif. (His statement: 'al-murawnaq') 'al-muwaththaq' is preferable and more suitable. And 'al-murawnaq' means adorned. The author recited in his commentary the poet's saying: 'So this one has the rawnaq of the line alone, and this one has the rawnaq of the line and the king.' And Ibn Sahl in the same meaning – and how subtle it is: [from al-Kamil] 'The water of bliss has flowed on his face, so the course of water blackened in the coals. The letters of poetry were written on his cheeks, what his eyes had committed in the souls. So you see the sins of his eyelids on his cheek, but on them is the rawnaq of good deeds.'
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the commentary on the introductory lines of the poem 'al-Sullam al-Murawnaq'. The commentator first explains the word 'haaka' (هَاكَ) in detail, noting that it is an action noun (ism fi'l) meaning 'take'. He gives its morphological variations: for masculine, feminine, dual, and plural, and notes that the 'kaf' can be replaced by a hamza. He cites the Qur'anic verse 69:19 as evidence for this usage. Then he explains the phrase 'min usulihi' (from its foundations), clarifying that 'min' here is partitive, meaning 'take some rules from its rules'. He then defines 'funuunihi' (its branches) as the particulars of those rules. Finally, he defines 'al-murawnaq' as 'beautified' or 'adorned', citing a lexical source and a poetic example to support the meaning. He also notes a variant opinion that 'al-muwaththaq' (the strengthened) might be more appropriate.

أُصُولٍ

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Precise definition of terms in logical texts is not pedantry; it establishes the foundational categories on which all subsequent reasoning rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a key term in the opening of another classical text (e.g., Ibn Ajurrum's al-Muqaddima al-Ajurrumiyya) and analyze its grammatical and lexical features as the commentator does in this passage.
?
Explain how the distinction between 'usul' and 'funun' in logic parallels the structure of a science like fiqh (usul al-fiqh vs. furu' al-fiqh). Provide a concrete example of a rule in usul al-fiqh and a branch ruling derived from it.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Metaphor and Supplication in Scholarly Introductions
Commentary explains metaphor and supplication in the introduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the use of reinforced metaphor (isti'ara murashshaha) in Islamic scholarly introductions and interpret the author's supplication for sincerity.

Core Idea
Scholarly introductions use metaphor and supplication to frame purpose and hope.

Commentators often begin by unpacking the author's metaphorical language and personal supplications. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's poem does exactly that: it explains the metaphor 'sky of logic' as a reinforced metaphor for difficult issues, and then clarifies the author's prayer for sincerity and benefit. Understanding this front matter helps you appreciate the humility and pedagogical intent behind classical Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphor
Locate the phrase 'sama''ilm al-mantiq' in the Arabic. The commentator says it is a metaphor (isti'ara) for the difficult issues of logic. Notice that the metaphor is 'reinforced' (murashshaha) by mentioning 'ascent' (ruqiy) in the previous line. This reinforcement strengthens the metaphorical link.
Reinforced metaphor is a term from balagha; the mention of ascent relates to the sky.
2
Analyze the supplication
Read the couplet where the author hopes the work is 'pure' (khalisan) and 'not shrinking' (laysa qalisa). The commentator glosses 'qalisa' as 'munqabidan' (shrinking), meaning lacking full sincerity. Understand that the author's hope is for complete sincerity for Allah's sake.
The word 'qalisa' is explained specifically in context.
3
Examine the circumstantial phrase
The phrase 'ila al-mutawwalaati yahtadi' is analyzed as a circumstantial phrase (hal) referring to the pronoun in 'khalisan'. This shows that the benefit of the work is guiding the beginner to longer texts. See how the commentator parses the grammar to extract meaning.
This grammatical analysis is typical in sharh works.
4
Connect to the broader purpose
Reflect on why authors begin with such metaphors and supplications. They humble themselves, clarify the scope (difficult issues), and seek divine sincerity and benefit. This frames the work as both a serious scholarly endeavor and a act of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على مقدمة الناظم وَأَرَادَ بِـ سَمَاءِ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ : مَسَائِلَهُ الصَّعْبَةَ، وَفِيهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ مُرَشَحَةٌ بِذِكْرِ الرُّقِي (١) . قَوْلُهُ : (۱۳) واللهَ أَرْجُو أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصَا لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيم لَيْسَ قَالِصا (١٤) وَأَنْ يَكُونَ نَافِعَا لِلْمُبْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى المُطَوَّلَاتِ يَهْتَدِي قَوْلُهُ : (لَيْسَ قَالِصاً أَيْ: لَيْسَ مُنْقَبِضاً (۲) عَنْ دَرَجَةِ(۳) الإِخْلَاصِ، وَجُمْلَةُ إِلَى المُطَوَّلَاتِ يَهْتَدِي : فِي مَحَلَّ نَصْبِ حَالٍ مِنْ ضَمِيرِ خَالِصَا (٤)) .
Discourse on the author's introduction: And he meant by 'the sky of the science of logic' its difficult issues, and in it there is a reinforced metaphor by mentioning ascent (ruqiy). His statement: (13) 'And I hope to Allah that it be pure for His noble face, not shrinking.' (14) 'And that it be beneficial to the beginner, by it he is guided to the lengthy works.' His statement: 'laysa qalisa' means: not shrinking from the degree of sincerity, and the sentence 'ila al-mutawwalaati yahtadi' is in the accusative case as a circumstantial phrase referring to the pronoun in 'khalisan'.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the author's introduction. The commentator explains that the phrase 'sama''ilm al-mantiq' (the sky of the science of logic) is a metaphor (isti'ara) for the difficult issues (masa'il al-sa'ba) of logic, and the metaphor is 'reinforced' (murashshaha) by the mention of 'ascent' (ruqiy) in the previous line. Then the commentator turns to the next couplet (13-14) where the author expresses his hope that his work be pure (khalisan) for Allah's face and not 'qalisa' (shrinking from sincerity). He glosses 'qalisa' as 'munqabidan' (shrinking). The commentator also notes that the phrase 'ila al-mutawwalaati yahtadi' (by it he is guided to the lengthy works) functions as a circumstantial phrase (hal) referring to the pronoun in 'khalisan', explaining that the benefit of the work is that it guides the beginner to longer texts.

Key Terms
سَمَاءِ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ
اسْتِعَارَةٌ مُرَشَحَةٌ
قَالِصا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Reinforced metaphors and supplications in scholarly introductions convey the work's scope, difficulty, and the author's reliance on Allah for sincerity and benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another reinforced metaphor (isti'ara murashshaha) in a classical Islamic text (e.g., a line from al-Busiri's Qasidat al-Burda) and explain how the reinforcement works by identifying the related word (like 'ascent' reinforcing 'sky').
?
Write a short supplication (du'a) in Arabic for your own scholarly work, mimicking the structure: ask for sincerity (ikhlas) and benefit (naf') for beginners, using a metaphor if possible.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Three Opinions on Studying Logic and the Prohibitionist Arguments
Author presents three opinions and details prohibition proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on studying logic and reconstruct the two proofs for its prohibition as presented by Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī.

Core Idea
Logic's permissibility debate hinges on its Greek origins and the Salaf's practice.

The study of logic has been a matter of debate among Islamic scholars. In this passage, the author outlines three main opinions: prohibition, recommendation, and permissibility for the advanced student. It then details the two key arguments from the prohibition camp. Understanding these positions helps you see how scholars weigh the benefits and harms of an intellectual discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Look for the three statements on permissibility. The passage mentions Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī prohibiting, a group saying it should be studied (yambaghi an yu'l), and the well-known correct opinion of permissibility for the complete practitioner of the Sunnah and Quran.
The second opinion is incomplete in the text but likely means recommended.
2
Locate the two proofs for prohibition
Find the two arguments explicitly given by the prohibitionists: (1) logic originates from philosophers with corrupt beliefs, so engaging deeply endangers one's creed; (2) the Companions and early Muslims did not study it, so it cannot be necessary.
These proofs appear after 'استدل القائل بالمنع بدليلين'.
3
Analyze the response to the chapter heading
The commentator notes that the author's chapter title only mentions 'permissibility', yet includes the disagreement. The response is that the heading implies an omitted 'waw' covering prohibition and recommendation as well.
This shows a technical clarification of the text.
4
Understand the wisdom behind knowing the ruling
The commentator explains that a student must know the ruling before acting, to avoid prohibited/disliked acts and to engage in obligatory/recommended ones. This underscores the importance of fiqh in every discipline.
This is a general principle: action requires knowledge of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ فَصْلٌ فِي جَوازِ الاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ
(١٥) وَالخُلْفُ فِي جَوَازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ أَقْوَالٍ :
(١٦) فَابْنُ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَاوِي حَرَّمَا، وَقَالَ قَوْمٌ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْلَ (١٧) وَالقَوْلَةُ المَشْهُورَةُ الصَّحِيحَهُ جَوَازُهُ لِكَامِلِ الـ (۱۸) مُمَارِسِ السُّنَّةِ وَالكِتَابِ لِيَهْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ
ذَكَرَ فِي هَذِهِ الأَبْيَاتِ حُكْمَ الشَّارع فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ؛ لِيَكُونَ طَالِبُهُ عَلَى بَصِيرَةٍ فِي حُكْمِهِ. وَالأَقْوَالُ الثَّلَاثَةُ ذَكَرَهَا الزَّرْكَشِيُّ : فَالقَوْلُ الأَوَّلُ : بِتَحْرِيمِهِ، عَزَاهُ لِابْنِ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَوِيِّ،
فَصْلٌ فِي جَوازِ الاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ تَرْجَمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ لِلْجَوَازِ، وَذَكَرَ فِي دَاخِلِ التَّرْجَمَةِ الْخِلَافَ. وَيُجَابُ عَنْهُ : بِأَنَّ فِي كَلامِهِ حَذْفَ «الْوَاوِ» مَعَ مَا عَطَفَتْهُ ؛ أَيْ : فِي جَوَازِ الاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ وَحُرْمَتِهِ وَنَدْبِهِ. قَوْلُهُ : (لِيَكُونَ طَالِبُهُ عَلَى بَصِيرَةٍ فِي حُكْمِهِ) لأنَّهُ قَدِ انْعَقَدَ الْإِجْمَاعُ عَلَى أَنَّهُ لا يَجُوزُ لأَحَدٍ الْإِقْدَامُ عَلَى أَمْرٍ حَتّى يَعْلَمَ حُكْمَ اللهِ تَعَالَى فِيهِ، وَلأنَّ الطَّالِبَ مَعَ جَهْلِهِ رُبَّمَا يَقَعُ فِيمَا عَسَاهُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَمْنُوعاً أَوْ مَكْرُوهاً فَإِذَا عَلِمَهُ أَحْجَمَ، أَوْ يُعْرِضُ عَمَّا عَسَاهُ أَنْ يَكُونَ وَاجِباً أَوْ مَنْدُوباً فَإِذَا عَلِمَهُ أَقْدَمَ، وَزَادَ نَشَاطاً وَرَغْبَةً. قَوْلُهُ : (فَالْقَوْلُ الْأَوَّلُ : بِتَحْرِيمِهِ، عَزَاهُ لِبْنِ الصَّلَاحِ وَالنَّوَوِيِّ) اسْتَدَلَّ الْقَائِلُ بِالْمَنْعِ بِدَلِيلَيْنِ : الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ مِنْ عُلُومِ الْفَلاسِفَةِ، وَهُمْ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْعَقَائِدِ الْفَاسِدَةِ، يُوشِكُ أَنَّ مَنِ اسْتَغْرَقَ هِمَّتُهُ فِي عُلُومِهِمْ أَنْ يَسْتَرِقَّهُ فِي بَعْضِ الْعَقَائِدِ. الثَّانِي : أَنَّ الصَّحَابَةَ وَمَنْ فِي مَعْنَاهُمْ مِنَ السَّلَفِ الصَّالِحِ لَمْ يَشْتَغِلُوا بِهِ، وَلَوْ كَانَ مُحْتَاجاً إِلَيْهِ مَا أَغْفَلُوهُ.
His saying: Chapter on the permissibility of occupying oneself with it. (15) And the disagreement regarding the permissibility of occupying oneself with it is upon three statements: (16) Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī declared it forbidden, and a group said it should [be studied], (17) and the well-known correct statement is its permissibility for a complete practitioner of the Sunnah and the Book, to be guided by it to correctness. He mentioned in these verses the ruling of the Lawgiver regarding this discipline, so that its student may be upon insight regarding its ruling. And the three statements were mentioned by al-Zarkashī: The first statement: its prohibition, attributed to Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī. Then a chapter on permissibility: the author translated for permissibility, and he mentioned within the translation the disagreement. And it is responded to: that in his speech there is deletion of the 'wāw' along with what it conjoins; i.e., regarding the permissibility of occupying oneself with it, its prohibition, and its recommendation. His saying: 'so that its student may be upon insight regarding its ruling' because there is consensus that no one may proceed upon a matter until he knows Allah's ruling regarding it, and because the student in his ignorance may possibly fall into something that may be prohibited or disliked, then if he knows it he refrains, or he may abandon something that may be obligatory or recommended, then if he knows it he proceeds, and increases in activity and desire. His saying: 'The first statement: its prohibition, attributed to Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī.' The one who said prohibition argued with two proofs: The first: that it is from the sciences of the philosophers, and they are people of corrupt beliefs, it is likely that whoever devotes his concern to their sciences will be enslaved in some beliefs. The second: that the Companions and those like them from the pious predecessors did not occupy themselves with it, and if it were needed they would not have neglected it.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the legal ruling (ḥukm shar'ī) concerning the study of the science of logic. The commentator lists three opinions: (1) absolute prohibition, attributed to Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī; (2) a group said it should be studied (the text is incomplete but likely means it is recommended); (3) the well-known correct opinion is that it is permissible for one who is fully grounded in the Quran and Sunnah, serving as a tool for reaching truth. The commentator then explains that the poet mentioned these rulings so the student is aware of them. He notes that although the poet's chapter heading says 'permissibility', he included the disagreement, and the response to any objection is that the heading implies deletion of the conjunctive wāw, meaning the chapter covers permissibility, prohibition, and recommendation. Then he elaborates on the wisdom of knowing the ruling, invoking consensus that action requires knowledge. Finally, he details the two proofs of the prohibition camp: first, logic originates from pagan philosophers, posing a danger of corrupting belief; second, the Companions and early Muslims did not engage in it, indicating it was not needed. These arguments are typical of the debate on the legitimacy of logic in Islam.

Key Takeaway
The debate on logic's permissibility is framed by its origin in Greek philosophy and the practice of the early Muslims, with the moderate view allowing it for those already grounded in primary sources.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Ruling on Studying Logic: Mandub, Not Muharram
Author establishes the legal ruling (hukm) of studying logic as recommended (mandub).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the final juristic ruling on studying logic according to this passage and refute the two main arguments for its prohibition.

Core Idea
Logic is recommended because it aids religious sciences, but is not itself obligatory.

This passage tackles a heated debate: is studying logic lawful or forbidden? The commentator refutes the prohibition and establishes that the correct ruling is recommendation (mandub). Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for prohibition
The text says those who prohibit logic either do so out of ignorance (an invalid judgment) or knowing its true nature (in which case their words refer only to corrupted forms). Extract these two possibilities from the Arabic.
The author argues that absolute prohibition is not a valid statement.
2
Refute the two proofs against logic
The text mentions two proofs for prohibition: (1) logic is a foreign science, and (2) logic is innate and thus unnecessary. The author refutes both: many foreign sciences (medicine, astronomy) were adopted; and what is innate is natural inference, not technical terminology.
3
Find the second opinion and its justification
Locate the line: 'The second opinion: that it is recommended (mandub).' The author declares this the truth because logic is a means to acquiring recommended and obligatory sciences.
Support from al-Ghazali, Ibn Arafa, al-Sanusi.
4
Explain why logic is not obligatory (wajib)
The text clarifies that acquiring obligatory knowledge (e.g., creed) does not depend on logic; countless scholars attained it without it. Thus, logic is not a prerequisite for wajib knowledge, so it remains mandub.
This refutes the claim that logic is wajib.

Source Text

فصل في جواز الاشتغال به
(۱) وَعَزَاهُ السُّيُوطِيُّ لِأَكْثَرِ أَهْلِ العِلْم مِنَ المُحَدِّثِينَ وَالفُقَهَاءِ .
قَالَ بَعْضُ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ : فِيهِ نَظَر، بَلِ القَوْلُ بِتَحْرِيمِهِ عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ لَا يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعَدَّ قَوْلاً ، لأَنَّهُمْ : - إِنْ قَالُوا ذَلِكَ مَعَ جَهْلِهِمْ بِهِ وَبِمَنْفَعِتِهِ ، فَهُوَ حُكْمٌ عَلَيْهِ قَبْلَ تَصَوُّرِهِ، فَيُكُونُ بَاطِلاً . - وَإِنْ كَانَ مَعَ عِلْمِهِمْ بِذَلِكَ، تَعَيَّنَ - كَمَا عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الأَئِمَّةِ - حَمْلُ كَلَامِهِمْ عَلَى مَا وَرَاءِ القَدْرِ المُحْتَاجِ إِلَيْهِ ؛ الَّذِي لَخَصَهُ أَئِمَّةُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ وَتَعَاطُوهُ وَوَصَّوا بِالمُحَافَظَةِ عَلَيْهِ؛ إِذْ لَا شُبْهَةَ تُوهِمُ حُرْمَتَهُ ، قصارة.
وكلا الدليلين في غاية السُّقوط :
أمَّا الأَوَّل : فإِنَّ كثيراً من علوم الفلاسفة نقلت إلى الإسلام، وأثبتتها الأئمة على سبيل الوجوب أو الندب كـ: التوقيت، والطب، والحساب.
والثاني : أنَّ الـمـنـطـق مركوز في الطباع بنقل الإجماع؛ إذ حـاصلـه اسـتـدلال بـوجـود أحـدِ المتلازمين على الآخر، وبعدمه على عدمه، أو استدلال بوجود أحد المتعاندين على عدم الآخر، أو بعدمه على وجوده، وهذا لا ينكره عاقل، وحينئذ فليس للفلاسفة إِلَّا مجرد النسبة والاصطلاح، ولا جرم أنَّ مَن له ذهن سليم لا يحتاج إلى الاصطلاحات المنطقيَّة، كما لا يحتاج العربي إلى تعلم اصطلاحات العربية .
قوله : (وَعَزَاهُ السُّيُوطِيُّ . • إلخ) قد صرح رضي الله تعالى عنه في النّقاية» و«الإتقان» بتحريم المنطق، وراجعه في ذلك ناصر السُّنَّة أبو عبد الله المغيلي نظماً ونثراً حتّى رجع عن ذلك، كما قاله الفقيه سيدي أحمد بابا، ويدلُّ لهذا مدحه إيَّاه في نظم «العقود (۲).
قوله : بَعْضُ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ عنى به الهلالي. قوله : (مَعَ جَهْلِهِمْ بِهِ كما يحكى أنَّ ابن الصلاح اشتغل به نحواً من عشرين عاماً، فلم يحصل فيه على طائل، فرجع عنه وحرَّمه . قوله : (وَوَصَّوا بِالمُحَافَظَةِ عَلَيْهِ) قد نقل الولي الصالح السنوسي عن الإمام الأبي في شرحه على مسلم»: «أَنَّ الشَّيخ ابن عرفة كان يوصي تلامذته عليه، ويؤكد الوصية».
فَيَكُونُ الخِلافُ فِي حَالٍ، لَا حَقِيقِيًّا . شيخ الثاني على السلم المرونق (۲) - وَالقَوْلُ الثَّانِي : أَنَّهُ مَنْدُوبٌ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ مُقْتَضَى كَلَام الغَزَالِيُّ (١) ، وَابْنِ عَرَفَةَ فِي [أ/ []/٦] نَقْلِ الأَبي عَنْهُ ، وَالسَّنُوسِيِّ وَغَيْرِهِمْ، وَهُوَ الحَقُّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَسِيلَةٌ إِلَى تَحْصِيلِ العُلُومِ الَّتِي مِنْهَا مَنْدُوبٌ وَوَاجِبٌ .
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ وَاجِباً مَعَ أَنَّهُ يُتَوَصَّلُ بِهِ إِلَى العِلْمِ الوَاجِبِ كَالعَقَائِدِ؛ لِأَنَّ تَحْصِيلَ العُلُوم الوَاجِبَةِ لَيْسَ مَوْقُوفاً عَلَيْهِ ؛ لِحُصُولِهَا لِمَنْ لَا يُحْصَى كَثْرةٌ مِنَ العُلَمَاءِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يَتَعَاطَوْهُ ، فَلَيْسَ حُصُولُ العُلُومِ الكَسْبِيَّةِ (٢) بِدُونِهِ مِنْ خَوَارِقِ العَادَاتِ (٣) ، بَلْ هُوَ شَائِعٌ كَثِيرٌ ( ٤ ) كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ ، خِلَافاً لِمَا ذَكَرَهُ الشَّيْخُ اليُوسِيُّ فِي حَاشِيَتِهِ»(٥) ، وَلِمَا فِي شَرْحِ المَطالِعِ ) مِنَ الحُكْمِ بِوُجُوبِ مَعْرِفَتِهِ ، ) قصارة (1)
وقال الغزاليُّ : مَن لا معرفة له بالمنطق لا يوثق بعلمه، نقله زكريا في شرح إيساغوجي وقال أبو على الماكري : هذا الفن لا يعطيه الله بكماله إلَّا لَمَن أحبَّ مِن أوليائه .
قوله : ( فَيَكُونُ الخِلافُ فِي حَالٍ أي: لفظيًّا، ومعناه : أنَّه لو اطلع عليه اليوم المحرمون له من حيث إنَّه يشوش الفكر، ونحو ذلك ؛ لأجازوه كما أنه لو بقي على حاله الأول لحرَّمه المجيزون له؛ لاشتماله على بعض الأمور المفسدة للعقائد كقولهم فيه بالجنس المنفرد، وتمثيلهم له بالعقل المفارق الذي هو عند القائل به جوهر مجرَّد، وتقسيمهم له إلى عشرة عقول؛ التي لا يقسمها لذلك إلَّا الفلاسفة، وتمثيلهم أيضاً للكلِّيّ الذي لا نهاية له بحركة الأفلاك إلى غير ذلك، وحيث هذبه الأئمة زالتِ العِلَّة، فيزول معلولها وهو التحريم. (1)
قوله : (فِي نَقْلِ الأَبي عَنْهُ أي : لا في «منطقه»، فإنَّه صرَّح فيه بالوجوب.
قوله : (وَغَيْرِهِمْ) أي: مِنَ المتكلمين والأصوليين وكثير من الفقهاء.
قوله : كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الشَّيْخُ السَّنُوسِيُّ) في شرح منطق ابن عرفة معترضاً تعبيره بالوجوب
Chapter on the permissibility of engaging in it. (1) Al-Suyuti attributed it to most scholars from the Traditionists and jurists. One of the researchers said: There is consideration in this; rather, the statement of its absolute prohibition should not be considered a statement, because they - if they said that while being ignorant of it and its benefit, then it is a judgment upon it before its conception, so it is false. And if it was while knowing that, then it is necessary - as many imams held - to interpret their words as (referring to) beyond the needed amount that the imams of Ahl al-Sunnah have summarized, practiced, and recommended preserving; for there is no ambiguity that would suggest its prohibition. (decorative line) And both proofs are extremely weak: As for the first, many of the philosophers' sciences were transferred to Islam, and the imams affirmed them as obligatory or recommended, such as: timing, medicine, and arithmetic. And the second: that logic is ingrained in human nature by transmission of consensus; for its essence is inference from the existence of one of two mutually implying things to the other, and from its non-existence to its non-existence, or inference from the existence of one of two mutually exclusive things to the non-existence of the other, or from its non-existence to its existence. This no sane person denies. Then the philosophers have nothing but mere classification and terminology. Indeed, one with a sound mind does not need logical terminologies, just as an Arab does not need to learn Arabic terminologies. He said: '(He attributed it to al-Suyuti... etc.)' He, may Allah be pleased with him, explicitly stated in 'al-Nuqaya' and 'al-Itqan' the prohibition of logic, but Nasir al-Sunna Abu Abdullah al-Maghili debated him in poetry and prose until he retracted, as said by faqih Sidi Ahmad Baba, and this is indicated by his praise of it in the poem 'al-Uqud' (2). He said: 'One of the researchers' he meant al-Hilali. He said: '(with their ignorance of it)' as it is narrated that Ibn al-Salah engaged in it for about twenty years, but did not achieve any benefit, so he abandoned it and prohibited it. He said: '(and they recommended preserving it)' The righteous saint al-Sanusi narrated from Imam al-Abi in his commentary on 'Muslim': 'That Shaykh Ibn Arafa used to recommend his students to study it and emphasize the recommendation.' Then the disagreement is in form, not in reality. (2) The second opinion: That it is recommended (mandub) to study it, and this is implied by the words of al-Ghazali (1), and Ibn Arafa in [A/[]/6] as transmitted by al-Abi from him, and al-Sanusi and others, and it is the truth; because it is a means to acquiring sciences, some of which are recommended and some obligatory. And it is not obligatory despite being a means to obligatory knowledge like the creeds, because acquiring obligatory sciences is not dependent on it; as they are attained by countless scholars who did not practice it. So the attainment of acquired sciences without it is not a miracle, but it is widespread and abundant (4) as Shaykh al-Sanusi mentioned, contrary to what Shaykh al-Yusi mentioned in his gloss (5), and contrary to what is in 'Sharh al-Matali' of the ruling of the obligation of knowing it. (decorative line) (1) Al-Ghazali said: Whoever has no knowledge of logic cannot be trusted in his knowledge, narrated by Zakariyya in his commentary on Isaghuji. And Abu Ali al-Makari said: This art Allah does not grant its perfection except to whom He loves from His saints. He said: '(then the disagreement is in form)' i.e., verbal. Its meaning: that if the prohibitors today were to examine it in terms that it confuses the mind, etc., they would permit it, just as if it had remained in its original state, the permitors would prohibit it because it contained some corrupting matters for the beliefs, like their statements about the separate genus, and their example of it with the separate intellect which, according to those who hold it, is a simple substance, and their division of it into ten intellects, which only the philosophers divide as such, and their example of the infinite universal with the movement of the spheres, etc. And when the imams purified it, the cause ceased, so its effect, which is prohibition, ceases. (1) He said: '(in the transmission of al-Abi from him)' i.e., not in his 'Mantiq', for he explicitly stated in it the obligation. He said: '(and others)' i.e., from the theologians, legal theorists, and many jurists. He said: 'as Shaykh al-Sanusi mentioned' in his commentary on the logic of Ibn Arafa, objecting to his expression of obligation.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a front_matter discussion on the legal ruling (hukm) of studying the science of logic (mantiq). It begins by stating that a chapter on the permissibility of engaging in logic, and notes that al-Suyuti attributed the prohibition to most scholars. The commentator then presents a critique from 'one of the researchers' (identified as al-Hilali) who argues that the claim of absolute prohibition is invalid. The researcher gives two scenarios: either those who prohibit logic do so out of ignorance of its nature and benefit, which is a judgment without proper conception and thus false; or they know it well, in which case their statements must be interpreted as referring only to the excessive philosophical elements that are corrupt, not to logic itself as purified by Sunni imams. The passage then refutes two arguments for prohibition: first, that logic is a foreign science — but many foreign sciences like astronomy and medicine have been adopted; second, that logic is innate — but this is a straw man, as what is innate is natural reasoning, not technical terminology. The commentary then explains that al-Suyuti initially prohibited logic but retracted after debate. It also narrates that Ibn al-Salah practiced logic for twenty years with no success and then prohibited it, but this is an exception. The passage then asserts that the real disagreement is verbal, not substantive: if logic is purified of philosophical corruptions (like the separate intellect and ten intellects), even the prohibitors would permit it. The second opinion is that logic is recommended (mandub), supported by Ghazali, Ibn Arafa, al-Sanusi, and others. This is declared the truth because logic is a means to both recommended and obligatory sciences. However, it is not itself obligatory because many scholars acquired obligatory knowledge without formal logic. The claim that acquiring knowledge without logic is a miracle is refuted; it is actually common. The passage ends with al-Ghazali's strong statement that one who does not know logic cannot be trusted in his knowledge, indicating its importance. The commentator clarifies that the apparent disagreement is merely about the form of logic, not its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is studying logic not obligatory (wajib) despite being a means to obligatory sciences?
A) Because it is a foreign science and therefore cannot be obligatory.
B) Because it is innate in human nature and thus not in need of study.
C) Because many scholars attained obligatory knowledge without ever studying logic.
D) Because the philosophers corrupted it with false doctrines.

Key Takeaway
The correct legal ruling on studying purified logic is that it is recommended (mandub), not obligatory (wajib), because it aids but is not necessary for acquiring religious knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a contemporary student who wants to study logic to better understand theological arguments. Using the reasoning from this passage, evaluate whether their study is recommended, obligatory, or forbidden.
?
